Several things I disagree with. First, taste didn't change. People travel hundreds of kilometers to visit neoclassical downtowns, they pay hugh rents to live there, and shops want to be located in there. Second, even though I agree many cities are too car-centered, that doesn't really take away from beautiful buildings. Walking through Paris or Budapest you will realize both cities are very car-centered, but they still look beautiful. In fact, going to a classic European pedestrian shopping street, many buildings are clustered with company logos. Car or bike or foot, it's not the question here. The problem is we focus on productivity over beauty, ever since the industrialization. But now that the old beauty fades, we realize we need beauty for the piece of mind, we are no machines. And the neo-liberal argument doesn't really hold when we consider that people love to overpay to live in a nice area with neo-classical buildings, at least in Europe. So you say the price is based on location, but that's not the full story. People pay to live in a nice neighborhood. They have a car or public transport to get to the city centre, they definitely prefer a good-quality neighborhood over location. So if you want to make money, building good-looking, expensive houses should not be in your way, as long as the rest of the package fits.

What bothers me most about the ugliness of modern buildings is that it's not even that hard to make prettier buildings. I play Minecraft now and then and there are professional Minecrafters who make vids on how to make pretty buildings with very simple rules  like: - don't build a building in the shape of a box,  - makes sure the facade isn't flat and  - avoid large homogenous surfaces.   And then I look at modern buildings and they all make these basic mistakes. I don't get how professional architects can all be so incompetent.
I find that a similar trend is visible in other modern art forms. The main criterion seems to be innovation and to hell with appeal. Strangely enough, sometimes the best places to find pretty things are cheap shops. I bet that that's because people who shop there are unfamiliar with the dictates of modern art and simply buy what they like.
I'm. pretty sure that if builders were made to spend 5% of building costs on making a building prettier, this would have a huge impact. Ornaments don't have to be hand-made. As long as there is enough variation on different scales, a lot of repetition is possible.

Modernism was still focused on aesthetics, it just achieved it in different way. The neoclassical buildings are really just primitive solids with ornamentation attached, which is visible when the ornamentation is removed - the building itself is just an uninspired matchbox. Modernism was achieving aesthetics with geometry by using bevels, bezels, protrusions, extrusions, alcoves, and so on. It was also focused on environmental aesthetics that carried over to the commie blocks (at least in eastern Europe) with the idea of "Garden Cities". The only really depressing thing are the contemporary "habitation camps" that lack green spaces, room, sunlight, recreational facilities, playgrounds... things that make life bearable in the essence. And yes, the cause is the need to squeeze as much "living space" from acre as possible. That's why we came back to the idea of matchbox buildings just without the costly ornamentation.

As usual you are making too much false assumption. First fallacy is that ornamentation is expensive - it is not at it wasn't. Most 19th and early 20th century city buildings used mass produced plaster ornaments which were cheap. Secondly, in most places beatiful and traditional architecture of ordinary town houses does not contain a lot of ornaments and costly elements. For example Copenhagen Kartoffelr√¶kkerne - there is absolutly no expensive technique applied to those houses. The ornamentation is just a well designed masonry/brick patterns. Secondly - traditional buildings even big ones didn't have a luxury of mechanical ventilation or air conditioning, so they tend to be designed to be more passive and more sustainable then "supposedly" cheaper modern buildings.
I would also add survivorship bias to the picture. We know pre-modern architecture mostly from pretty, well-preserved historic urban centres, which are tourist destinations. Most of people in history lived in wooden shacks or at best, dirty, half-ruined, overpopulated tenement houses. Which mostly has been razed since then or renovated and rebuild to look like tourist Disneyland (like Prague`s famous Zlata Ulicka).
I just need to point out that people who already own a car, obviously, won't buy an apartment in a building without an option to park a car. And since a lot of the people who can afford an apartment already own a car - for you to sell an apartment, you need to think, where that car would go)
"Change in tastes," is a nice way of saying having no taste at all. Two traits I've become increasingly aware of in current times: setting and maintaining standards of any kind is seen as "bad," because it places responsibility on people, particularly people who fail to meet those standards (we'll just ignore the fact those failures are largely due to ignorance and indifference, not lack of ability). Secondly, "taste" is not something we are born with; it's something that must be developed and cultivated, and the majority of people today (including developers, architects, decision makers) lack the time and motivation: the end result, all we get is marketing and cheapness.

Enjoyed this vid! The interesting about ornamentation though is that when it's mass produced it becomes (like most things) much more cheaper. In the Victorian era many architectural snobs lamented just how affordable mass produced ornamentation had become - pediments, carving, banding, etc - and therefore had apparently lost it's class and meaning. I should imagine that most forms of ornament for brick and concrete even today are likely still affordable. But we live in an era of sleek/minimalist/low effort clothing, devices and such. Anything more is seen as fussy and most people, honestly, don't care, as our value systems are different from back then.

tl;dr Blame capitalism in general, not just neoliberal capitalism  You will find that the politics and economics of aesthetics are even more important than you have outlined in this video, if you compare, say, Gr√ºnderzeit-era Leipzig with Glasgow or Edinburgh tenements. Both are examples of load-bearing masonry buildings erected in the period of rapid industrial development and influx of people into the cities - yet the Scottish tenements are way more austere and less diverse, not designed plot by plot, like German tenements, but instead predominantly designed and built as entire blocks at once.  Similarly, long before the Soviet-style communism or war damage, the planned working-class estates of Manchester created drab, monotonous urban landscapes the rest of the world was yet to replicate - or strive to avoid. Anyone who considers the green planned post-war estates of Warsaw, Prague or Budapest ugly is cordially invited to join me on a guided tour around Manchester, focused on the numerous Victorian terraces that managed to escape 'slum clearances': certainly not what springs to mind when people talk about '19th-century architecture', even though it was THE experience of architecture of that time for most urban dwellers.  Yes, the red-brick and sandstone, tiled roofs and narrow streets are probably, by contrast, a sound testament to the huge problem of concrete weathering and even bigger problem of post-war car-centric urban planning, but otherwise there is no more tangible proof that 'ugly cities' were not conceived by Adolf Loos, Le Corbusier, Lenin or Robert Moses (pick your villain), though each of those men has a lot to answer for.

I actually like the minimalism of modernist architecture, I think the problem is that it's kind of been watered down into the corporate version of modernism. It's all the simplicity, without any of the understanding. Grey and white boxes of flat textures.  I think the best modern structures focus less on ornamentation, and more on shape, texture, and color contrast. They might not use as many colors, but they use two or three very bold ones. They might not have complex exteriors, but the combination of simple shapes casts shadows that highlight the form of the structure. They might have a lot of flat walls, but those walls are made of materials that cast of light and Shadow in a way that creates detail.  This is why something like Sydney Opera House or the Eiffel Tower work, while a Walmart does not.

Thank you for a good video. As a practising and teaching European architect, I‚Äôm usually quite worried about videos such as this but you went through some of the real reasons for this change. There are however, more reasons (naturally). One interesting aspect of modernism that often gets overlooked, is that as well as being more functional compared to older buildings at the time, the modern aesthetic was seen as a clear departure from the very hierarchical and conservative regimes of the past. Classical architecture was tightly linked to the use of power and opression by the few. Modernism represented democracy and equal rights writ large, which had an influence on the tastes at the time. It was fresh, optimistic, and coupled with the rapid technological development in the early 1900s, represented a bright future. Architects nor anybody else could see that the influence of monotonous cityscapes would be negative. It was in fact not possible to experience what this development would bring before it had already happened. The scale of building after the second world war was so great that it dwarfed everything that had come before. Thus we should be more understanding of the people who pushed for great modernist city projects at the time.  Architectural history is interestingly filled with people being shocked by classical buildings as well ‚Äì this is not just a modern occurance. In fact, the changing of architectural styles caused a lot of hate even with the evolution classical styles, more as a rule rather than as an exception. We nowadays view classical styles as a monolithic entity but they were all seen as quite great departures from each other at the time of their inception. People do actually get used to buildings and their tastes end up getting influenced by said buildings: there is data to show that at the age of 40-50 years, buildings of any given era are at their most hated, after that they start to become more liked, ultimately resulting in their lasting appreciation. We will see this happen to more and more modernist buildings in the coming decades. It should also be noted, that we also only see the very best of classical architecture nowadays (furthermore with modern amenities, plumbing, electricity, etc.) so we are all subject to great bias in that respect: most classical architecture has been lost to time as worse quality buildings were either torn down or became ruined long before present day. This similars development will happen to modernist buildings as well (if modern economic factors manage to not ruin this natural process).  I should also say, that as someone who has studied both classical and modern design techniques (and worked with both) they are not all that different and are actually beginning to merge. Classical architecture is coming back and becoming one with modernist principles at this very moment. We had this originally happen during the 1970-80s with postmodernism but that ended up as an unsuccessful trial (largely due to the modern economic drivers impacting construction and because architects were perhaps not able to bring about this ‚Äùmerging‚Äù so quickly). After that we‚Äôve had quite a few different approaches to modern design and cerain aspects of classical design but only now are we seeing an unironic utilization of classical techniques alongside modernist design techniques. This process will take time though because changes in the built environment and its culture are always slow. Luckily, the push towards sustainability is helping in this evolution, as quality construction and the possibility to repair buildings is becoming more and more important. Iteration, not just innovation, is getting recognized once more.  There are however, some worrisome caveats to this development, as we are currently also seeing the rise of authoritarian powers as well many populist ideologies within society. Many pro-classicist movements (in fact most of the big ones that I have seen in Europe and the US) are partly tied to the influence of such bad actors. People are right to request more beauty in their built environments but this should not come about through the seemingly easy answers that this type of political development often entails (eg. you should not simply ban ‚Äùmodern‚Äù architecture). Claims of ‚Äùmoving society back to it‚Äôs golden age‚Äù and the outright rejection of modern values should be clear signs for people to dig a little deaper and to not accept things at face value. People should understand that while architecture seems simple on the outside, the cultural and economic drivers that push it forward are in fact complex and should be respected as such. Without this respect, we risk pushing architecture backwards, and re-connecting classical architecture with it‚Äôs roots as a force for opression and for highlighting the status of the few.

In my opinion, the bigger problem, at least in the U.S., is when new buildings are built, especially skyscrapers, they're so expensive that the commercial tenants can only be luxury stores or big chains. That's why you don't have "neighborhoody" stores in new development areas, which adds to the sterilized feel. It hits differently when you have a restaurant, niche coffee shop, a pub, and/or a little convenience store vs. new buildings that just have a Starbucks and a Coach store.
Even in the higher end of the markets where the developer buys the land and develops it into profitable condo units, the developer most often chooses to spend more on the interior finishes to be able to sell the units while keeping their returns as high as possible. Exterior ornamentation in residential only really starts to appear in rentable buildings in higher income neighborhoods where the property owners are looking to keep that property for generations. They essentially invest into durable and sometimes custom fa√ßade work.

Fun fact: Czech communist regime actually made a rule to use a mandatory 4% of the budget for artworks and ornamentation on every public building project to make structures like commie blocks, subway stations and even the Magistr√°la more tolerable, and also to secure jobs for artists of that time. And it resulted in some interesting public artpieces, like traditional and modernist statues, mosaics, decorated windows, ceramic reliefs etc. Unfortunately, most of those artworks were never properly recognised and ofted destroyed and removed, because they represended the hated communist regime in the eyes of the public, even though many of the artists were actually disidents just trying to make a living and often tried to hide anti-regime messages in their abstract works.

He wants to hit the ground running, and start operating his business one day after he moves in. Two items:  1) your reference to men's suits being more appropriate today is itself an example of dead fashion/style evolution. In the 18th century, the Great Male Renunciation began. This was just a psychologist's fancy term for a socioeconomic cultural backlash against visual symbols of the ultrarich nobility's sartorial lavish style and excess. Every social or generational movement tends first reject what it resents most, is most familiar with, and seeks to embrace the opposite of what created it. The US move to the suburbs rejected the downtown lifestyle of the 1930s. The 90s to 2000s gentrification of zombie downtown areas was a generational reaction by the disaffected children who had to grow up in miserable, dysfunctional suburbia once they went to college, got decent paying jobs, and could choose to live near the hip, bohemian places they used to have to drive 30-60 minutes to go dancing, drinking, and attend warehouse concerts in their teens.  Same thing for the Jacobins who took up somber black colors and conservative cuts of clothing as an immediate visual statement opposing the nobility they hated. There's examples of this going back to the Franks. I remember one dynasty had a coronation ritual where the king would ride in a wagon drawn by two oxen. That plus his long hair were symbols of the king's connection to the prosperity of the land or something. But then due to external conflicts, maybe it was Charles Martel versus the Moors or something, the next dynasty undertook a PR campaign to discredit the old dynasty, laughing at their backward traditions of riding with dirty, slow oxen and wearing filthy long hair. No, the cool hip thing was close cropped hair (b/c the king was a warrior, and the knights had short hair to more easily wear helmets and prevent lice) and riding on HORSES ooooooh aaaaah.  2) Familiarity bias. At this point, everyone from customers to builders and architects have been born in, grown up in, and lived/worked around modernist architecture. Considering how badly most people pay attention to history, it's easy to understand that most of them simply have no clue that you can do anything else. The majority of humanity in every generation always prefers the safe consensus route. It's what worked for our risk averse ancestors who survived getting eaten by a saber-toothed tiger (which wasn't actually contemporaneous with modern humans, so j/k), and darn it, it'll work for boring cream wall paint schemes.  And since catering to the boring middle gets you the least risky chance of realizing a positive return on your investment, you go with mediocre. Works for fast food, movies, books, cars, everything that can be tested with focus groups.

I think there is another important economic factor, that you missed: EQUALITY. When the nice ornamental old buildings were built, the inequalities in most industrialized societies were MASSIVE  - because all the cool welfare redistributive stuff happened mostly AFTER all the "nice" buildings were built. So back in the day, a wealthy industrialist building his new villa or his new headquarter had access to ultra cheap labour. Today, with minimum wage laws etc, the cost of building has increased for a wealthy industrialist, because now the lower 99% has fought for fair wages, maternal leave, sick leave, no more child labour, no more 6 day workweeks... Because our society has become more egalitarian, things that used to get done by cheap labour have become relatively more expensive - so you think twice if you really can/want to afford all those nice details (even if they match your taste), because you can't hire day laborers anymore for an apple a day.

I really did appreciate your video defending communist block apartments; it changed my mind, which I think owing to the Cold War propaganda of my youth made me conflate them with failed US public housing projects of the 1970s, which is an unfair comparison (and glides right over the systemic racism in the USA that can't easily be disentangled from the plight of US public housing projects).  I used to like the perspective & critique of James Howard Kunstler and his books The Geography of Nowhere and Home from Nowhere.  Those books are still fine, but Kunstler himself has become increasingly more right-wing and almost reactionary and his racism is more nakedly apparent.  Modernism is a very mixed bag.  I love the Empire State Building and Rockefeller Center and the Eiffel Tower.  But people do find the broadly copied "International style" (big glass boxes) souless and uninspiring.  More locally, I'm frustrated that in our downtown of Houston, Texas, we have these luxury loft apartments that are more an investment vehicle with sky high rents that no reasonable working person, or even middle class person can really afford to live in.  And many downtown restaurants struggle and close because there's just not enough foot traffic downtown to keep them in business the way there could be if the rents were actually affordable and we cared about cultivating walkable urban community in the downtown spaces....we do have our rudimentary light rail but we only have 2 lines and only one of them gets reasonably close to the main domestic airport (Houston Hobby); there's the city busses but they're notoriously unreliable and difficult to navigate.  The Dallas-Fort Worth Metroplex is way ahead of Houston in terms of public rail transit; I'm quite jealous of what they have.  We do have beautiful, walkable islands in and around Houston but there's so much car-dependent hellscape surrounding everything else in between them.  We have a built up, walkable "town center" type market in my suburb of Sugar Land, and it is nice, but it feels like walking around a movie set....it's not part of an integrated whole with the rest of the surrounding landscape....and they have similar ludicrously expensive luxury apartments that ordinary folk have no hope of affording to live in.
I fear it will require a crisis like a modern equivalent to the bronze age collapse before we abandon car centric city planning and cheap shitty 5 over one housing.

Modernism is attacked by our post modern age both rightfully and wrongfully. These beautiful 19th century buildings we admire as beautiful weren't meant for us, because behind them were dirty mouldy shaques made for ordinary people. So old art was very elitist and exclusive. Modernism tore down the old mansions and shaques and built colourless boring monotonous buildings, because it combined classical ornamentation with inequality and vulgar exhibition of wealth. So modernist minimalism had the meaning of reducing a building to its core, so that many buildings can be built for everyone, and the ornamentation that symbolised social inequality would be erased. Simplicity was used to express democracy and economy. The modernist building's beauty was supposed to lie in in its lack of beauty. So we went from one extreme to the other. When post modernism saw modernist architecture,it saw its apparent ugliness and appreciated the beauty of the mansions of the past, but in the same time forgetting all about the shaques. Like 19th century people moved from palaces to housing projects in the 20th century. But the truth lies in the middle, the ideal city isn't Rio de Janeiro nor Irkutsk, we should appreciate both beauty and art, and also equality and democracy, avoiding both elitism and totalitarianism.
Hello. Architect here. I liked this video a lot because it goes deeper than the usual "modern architecture is all bland and soulles boxes", but it just scratches the surface of the matter (as a 10 minute video should do). I would add some key points to the discussion:  - We live in an industralized world. Mass producing ornaments most of the time produces cheap and tacky looking plastic objects. Just look at neoclassical villas in russia. Instead, focus now is on producing modular construction parts that can be assembled at a high technological level, to improve the building performance, comfort, and ease of construction. Ornaments were once fabricated by artisans and craftsmen, which would be not suitable in today's economy.  -  Construction times. Often times in these discussion people compare buildings that took decades and sometimes century to be built, like palaces and churches, to skyscrapers and public buildings which usually take 2-3 years. In the modern economy it wouldn't be sustainable to have construction sites lasting decades, and modern buildings are made to be replaced in 50-100 years, because the need of society are rapidly changing. With notable exceptions, as the Sagrada Familia in spain.  - There was actually a movement trying to get back ornament into architecture, and it partially succedeed, which is Post modernism (in which we are arguably still today). The results were often tacky and weird, but it was great to open up discussions about ornaments and historical connections in architecture again, which modernism was convinced were not a good thing (and even then, lots of modernist architectures had plenty of ornament). Now modern architecture is more free from the purely functionalist theory of the early XX century..  - Power dynamics, communication and liberalism. Since architectural language are an expression of their times, now there are just so many different kinds, as we live in a fragmented an multi faceted world. Many architectural languages that favored decoration were doing so in an attempt to impose a visual language to show power, richness and cultural supremacy, as in a lot of governmental neoclassical buildings, which were favored by colonialist and imperialist countries to show the supremacy of their culture, or in heavily decorated churches, to show the power and richness of the church and the importance of religion.   There are many more aspects to the discussion but i already wrote too much lol. I hope someone read all this lol

In addition on the "capitalism" part, I read or heard somewhere that there has been also a shift in the cost repartition in building construction. In the "old days" the materials where the main cost of a new building, labour cost was minimal since, well, no workers safety, low social standards etc. Thus the focus was naturally on the enhancement of the material, which was the bigger cost, thus high ornementation since it didn't cost much since labour price was low. But since the 20th century, the cost repartition shifted, now the materials are mostly cheap thanks to technical advancements, but labour is costly because human life and dignity had become a concern. Then it naturally discouraged any effort put into ornaments, and settled a more, let's say, "pragmatic" approach.

Ok, the Sydney Opera House is postmodern . postmodernism is the movement in opposition/development of standard modernism. For many reasons included here, but some more were: Modernism ignored everything previous (and  after) to it.  They were made on completely different principles. It was the first time industrial logic and consistency was applied when projecting and constructing buildings. So you could calculate things with much more precision. The buildings had no relation with their surroundings. You could have it anywhere in the world and apart from climate adjustments, very little would change.  Also the car centric planning was a good (decent) idea (for the time). You would need to have free and easy access to the ever increasing new metropoli. And what's the best way to go around? A private car, of course...That is...until the internet. The need to physically transport something or someone decreased rapidly when sending and receiving information in a myriad of different ways almost instantly became a widespread reality.

Much that is said about beauty and its importance in our lives ignores the minimal beauty of an unpretentious street, a nice pair of shoes or a tasteful piece of wrapping paper, as though those things belonged to a different order of value from a church by Bramante or a Shakespeare sonnet. Yet these minimal beauties are far more important to our daily lives, and far more intricately involved in our own rational decisions, than the great works of art which (if we are lucky) occupy our leisure hours. They are part of the context in which we live our lives, and our desire for harmony, fittingness and civility is both expressed and confirmed in them. Moreover, the great works of architecture often depend for their beauty on the humble context that these lesser beauties provide. Roger Scruton.

Hey,  I'm working on an academic essay on the topic of cultural devaluation in architecture, and I agree with you on a lot of things.  However, what you said about transport is not so true. In most European countries (especially in central Europe) the public transport network is well developed and most people travel by metro, tram, etc. These people actively walk around the city, so they can better absorb external influences.   The aim in architecture is not necessarily to influence people in such a way as to stop them and get their active attention. Rather, the goal in urban architecture should be to create a coherent, aesthetic and responsive environment, where you don't necessarily have to stop on your journey because there is always something to look at and wonder about. If the aim was to stop at every building, we would have a competition between buildings, so that there would be a constant contrast.   Yes, it's true that tastes change over time, but contemporary architecture changes with fashion, which in itself is not a good thing. Fashion changes but culture does not and unfortunately these old buildings and the ornamentation in or on them still represent the humanism of the European man. I could explain why, as this is one of the basic premises of the study, but then I would have to copy and paste the whole train of thought here and translate it.   I am against prefab architecture, its temporary necessity must be acknowledged, but we should not encourage similar endeavours in the future, at least without regard to the human experience.

it's true, architecture lost it's soul after WW2, don't try to defend modern ugly architecture
There are some developments in the UK which use classical, or local vernacular styles. The Prince of Wales (as he was then) caught a lot of flak from architects by promoting this, and condemning a lot of modern, mostly brutalism, architecture as oppressive. He famously described the new British Library as a place rhat looked as if that was where books were taken to be burned, not where they were to be cherished  Perhaps his most famous promotion was of the Poundbury, an experimental urban extension to Dorchester in the county of Dorset. It was designed as a high density, traditional urban environment with easy pedestrian access. It is mixed use, in that it's not purely residential, and there is some social housing. However, it is also not a car-free environment. Architecturally, it's often called "new classical", and it is high on the hate list of some architects who resent the role of the Prince of Wales, and view him as a backward looking architectural Philistine. However, it is immensely popular with those who live there, albeit they are a self-selecting group of course. The are a lot of local businesses and jobs within its boundaries, and the place is thriving in terms of both the economy and socially. Of course, it's very middle class, and the private houses are very expensive. It is meant to reach it's target population level of 6,000 in 2025.  Coincidentally, there is another Duchy of Cornwall development not too far from where I live, but that uses Cotswold vernacular architecture, and is essentially residential, albeit high density. I, personally live in a tiny Cotswold town, and whilst there was quite a lot of bland social housing built in the couple of decades after WW II, any new developments are essentially derived from Cotswold vernacular, albeit with more modern materials. That means essentially stone, or reconstituted stone, steep rooflines, shallow eaves and so on. In large part, this is because it is a conservation area subject to special rules. The developments are, again, high density, and on a small scale. Large scale housing developments in adjacent towns do not have such tight rules and have developed what might be called suburbs of urban housing, but they remain relatively walkable, especially compared to North America. Urban speed limits are generally being reduced to 20mph from 30mph (roughly 30 kmh from 50 kmh).  There is a lot of this so-called new urban stuff, not all of which features classical or vernacular architecture, but it is still the case that for most, something approaching a traditional house is the ideal property, albeit with modern amenities and insulation. However, cars are not going away, especially as this is a rural area, dotted with (mostly) small towns and villages.  To my mind, the architecture of residential properties in the UK has improved considerably in the last 30 years compared to the 40 years before that. I should add that when I talk of respecting traditional deign, I most certainly am not talking of something like mock Tudor, or a Disneyfication. You would not mistake a modern building for the 16th and 17th century housing in the centre of the town (most of which were once mostly tiny places lived in by local workers - to enter some now, is to enter a veritable Tardis in terms of how the space has been expanded).  In any event, I am happy enough in my reconstituted stone house, on the edge of the town, which is clearly modern (built in the 1990s), but is not completely bland.  Oh yes, I also have that very important thing; a drive large enough for a couple of cars and charging points...

To be fair it‚Äôs not housing that a speculative investment, it‚Äôs land. Houses by their very nature are a depreciating capital asset from the second they are built, it‚Äôs the underlying land/location that grows in value, considering that land has inherently fixed supply.  Liberalized zoning and implementing a Georgist land value tax would effectively remove land‚Äôs speculative status and would incentivize the most efficient utilization of land, allowing for cheaper housing.  Japan is a good example, as their housing prices have stayed basically the same for decades, and their houses are treated more as consumables rather than speculative investments, with most housing being basically worthless within 30 years of being built.

I'm in my last year of high school in Italy and we have to study Art History in my school. We're currently studying Dadaism (1916-1924), so I know a little bit about the subject. While it is true that the Eiffel Tower and the Empire State Building were made with the new materials of the time to try something new, they aren't the same kind of modernist art that you described. For many, modernism meant using new materials to build better ornaments. On the other hand, figures like Adolf Loos (who essentially foresaw the Rationalist movement of 1925) saw modernism as something purely functional. In his famous book "Ornament and Crime" (published in 1912), he stated architecture wasn't art because architecture had to follow functionality. That's why he designed his buildings with big white cubes and massive windows. The Empire State Building and the Eiffel Tower were modernist because of their materials but there was absolutely no functional reason for their architectural style. You also mentioned other modernist buildings as beautiful, like the fallingwater, but quite frankly, they're still ugly. They still only have generic cubic shapes. It's just that one of them has a waterfall that goes through it. The need to mass produce and create new housing is somewhat of an acceptable reason. However, there are many architectural companies and art schools (like the Bauhaus) who want more minimalism and are pushing to destroy our cities. Quite frankly, I absolutely hate modernist architecture and I wish there were laws against these bland buildings. German cities are incredibly ugly nowadays because of modernist architecture. Kassel is a good example. It was a dense beautiful city. Nowadays, it's a car infested city that's way less dense and way more ugly. Don't get me wrong, I understand why they used cheap materials and such to rebuild it after WW2. However, they also made sure it was rebuilt with the car in mind. There is no way that the new infrastructure they built was gonna be any less expensive than rebuilding the city the way it was before

Market-built housing serves the same purpose as state-directed housing.  More housing, even cheaply built housing, provides options for people to live in.  Markets, unlike governments, reward people for coming up with better ways to provide a better product or service, and in a population boom, market-built units are designed to provide maximum utility to the people who intend to live in them.  If capital is expensive, there's no reason to build to a 100-year design life, just like there's no reason to build a car that can last 100 years if it's going to cost twice as much. This is for the most part an informative video, but I really have one big problem. I feel you missed out one major factor that left out to contradict the populist assertions around architecture, but you also said something that exposed how incomplete your theory is without it.   Throughout the video you essentially described all the reasons why traditional forms of beauty vanished from architecture, but, at the end of the video you added the statement about other forms of modernist beauty anyway such as that terminal you prefer. This shows that today public buildings are still built in central pedestrian accessible places, that are not part of an mass accommodation constraint, are not intended as private commodity, but are still commonly derided or ignored by general public taste. This raises a central question, how do any of these factors then explain why virtually all these buildings are still modernist? These modern public buildings come in the form of parliament buildings like Hollyrood, stadiums, or university campuses and are often ostentatious in their own form and commonly overbudget.  The most credible way I can explain the pure persistence of modernism in places where none of these practical restrictions apply is that there is a hold possessed by modernism itself over the architectural establishment. Beginning at universities where modernism is exclusively taught, except in rare cases like at Notre Dame university, and then follows graduates into the world of work where modernisms continues to dominate the vast majority of expressive, special, and public architectural projects. From this I have to make the assertion that there is indeed an elitist disconnection between the taste of the architectural establishment and general public taste which you not only excluded but also characterised as a political straw man. There is a conversation to be had about the direction of our cities and the role of out of touch institutional cultures in acting above the overdue debate on the anti democratic development of our shared environment.

Interestingly enough, there was a time when car-centric infrastructure could be considered "beautiful" (though some might disagree) and had a lot of ornamentation, known as Googie Architecture. Sometimes referred to as Populuxe, or Doo Wop, Googie Architecture used unique geometric shapes, stars and atoms, and bright colors to catch the eye of passerby. Heavily inspired by the Atomic age and Space Race, these "The Jetsons" style buildings spread throughout California to the rest of the US, from the 1940s-50s to the early 70s. However, it began to lose popularity after that, for being "too flashy", the end of the Space Race, and not fitting in with the International Style popular in the 70s (Also, I noticed many Googie buildings don't have massive parking lots, as parking minimums weren't as strict back then, which probably helped fund the ornamentation).   Despite the lack of love for the style by architects and the undesirable buildings of the service industry, the architectural design is now seen as an important era of design, and several preservation efforts have saved many buildings from the wrecker. To this day, Googie plays an important aesthetic in retrofuturism.
It should not be forgotten that decorative elements, in general all protruding elements, are particularly prone to wear and require a lot of maintenance. Moreover, in areas such as Italy and Greece, earthquakes are frequent and rather intense. Decorative elements in the event of an earthquake can detach and fall on people in these cases. This is the same reason (earthquakes) why brick is no longer used in Italy. An interesting book on ornament in modernism is "Ornament and Crime" by Adolf Loos.

This is an overly simplistic video that attempts to condense the history of public buildings' and housing for the masses architecture in the western world over the last 2-3 centuries in a "modern architecture movements for dummies" type video of less than 10 minutes.  Not only that but it also attempts to include various historical and economic factors in the arguments presented on what drove architecture to become "ugly". Normally, including many factors in the discussion of topic would be a good thing provided care is exercised so that these factor are presented with the due accuracy and in the right context. Neither of these conditions is observed here. The over-simplistic manner of presentation and shallow reasoning just offends the viewer's intellect. For example, the modernist movement is associated in the video with the second world war housing destruction and the rise of population. While this may be partly true, the video fails to explain the core thesis of the movement and also fails to mention that modernist movement started a lot earlier and in fact coincided with the late neoclacissism for a good two decades or more. The lack of ornamentation in the modernist movement is also associated with cost cutting in comparison with the more costly building facades of neoclacissism. While there is an element of cost saving resulting from the lesser ornamentation, again the video fails to mention that modernist buildings were not entirely devoid of ornamental features (whether obviously visible on the facade/exterior or not), were not low quality buildings and, as a result of the latter, were not cheap to make - the opposite in fact.  While bringing up the argument of population rise (and hence the need for cheap buildings), the new materials and technologies again the video fails to mention that both of these factors did not necessarily mean that new buildings would have to be built necessarily cheaper: new materials although they did not necessarily cost less they did, however open more possibilities (nor were they entirely avoided in late neoclassical buildings) and shorter build times. The rise of population, while creating a greater need for cheap mass housing, also created more collective wealth and drove greater advances in technology and thus resulted in even more advanced materials, construction techniques and, most importantly, construction planning and building cost evaluation methods. And last, modernist buildings were of course modern, with all modern amenities of the time, none of which were available a few decades before and all of which cost more money. So it wasn't a case of choosing cheap versus expensive but on choosing on what and where to spend the money.  Moreover, the examples shown of "ugly" buildings versus not "ugly" buildings are just unfortunate, to say the least. The most important element of stylistic choice is simply that "beauty lies in the eye of the beholder" and also it is a by-product of culture and civilisation and as such, just like other things that result from these, it is learned and the public's eyes are trained on it and are trained to like it by various mechanisms not the least of which are habit, getting used to familiar things and so on. Both modernist and neoclassical buildings can be beautiful in their own right and in any school or style one can encounter ugly and beautiful examples. Beauty is a matter of traininng as well as being accustomed to contact with nature and cannot be considered in a stylistic vacuum, i.e. in a context that excludes the art of design itself as the determining factor, in a discourse where beauty is not presented or though in terms of aesthetic rules and objectives but on extraneous factors only, as is the case here. The video does not even begin by defining what it is that makes something beautiful or ugly and thus fails from the start.

Great points. I would add that the mentality of preserving (and sometimes rebuilding) heritage buildings is more prominent in older cities. In Canada we have a prime example of that in Montreal, where a lot of old, beautiful buildings are still preserved despite being in highly desirable locations, and hence highly sought after by speculators. By contrast, a much younger city like Vancouver does not fuss as much when it comes to tearing down heritage buildings to make room for boring square boxes to sell at inflated prices. I have seen buildings made of stone, full of history and character, being torn down at UBC to make room for boring, steel and glass boxes. The other thing is lighting. People back in the day were okay with small windows because artificial light seemed to replace natural light just fine. Nowadays natural light is all the rage (even though we keep building highrise after highrise blocking it...), so new buildings have to have huge windows to let in as much light as possible, leaving little room for decoration and character.

I actually see the proliferation of "ugly residential buildings" to be a kind of social progress. When people look at all the beautiful 1800s buildings in their city centers and imagine how good it all was back then, it's the most classic case of survivorship bias. Absolutely correct, but I think I need to add a fifth reason to the list, every bit as important one - you see, "beautiful" buildings built in e.g. 19th century were actually very simple. It was a brick box with nice decor outside, some wooden slabs between floors, a nice stair (I especially love wrought iron ones!) and a roof. They had no plumbing, no electrical installation, no elevators, and even when they started to get all those things in the early 20th century, it was all very simple by modern standards. These days, for example when a modern office building is being built, engineering systems like plumbing, fire suppression, electrical, comms, HVAC etc. can easily cost 30% or 40% of total cost to build a building.

Even when you do buy a home to have a nice place to live, there's a good chance you will still not go through the additional expenses of making it ornamental, because you know you're going to have to move eventually.  For more and more people, the idea of getting a job in one company and staying there for the rest of your professional life is just not as common.  There's a good chance you'll have to switch companies several times in search of better opportunities, and that will likely mean having to move.    Also, the idea of building a home you can pass on to your children is also something you see less and less.  Most likely your children would have to sell the house to move wherever their own professions and those of their romantic partners take them.  So why spruce up the place to your liking beyond the minimum, when some stranger is likely gonna end up with it eventually, who might not even like the ornaments you put in?

Honestly, and I'm probably going to get a bit hate for that, I think that late-stalinist Architecture found a nice middle path in it's fusion of modern Cunstructivist and old Classicist Elements. I have way too much knowledge on this topic, so here's a speedy summary: Revolution =Modern, Constr. = Modern -> Constr. = Revolution; Russian Civil War + NEP -> Population is sick of Revolution and rather cheap Constructivism, wants the nice buildings back; Stalin embraces the Classicist, but fused with modern Elements and less Prunk to have atleast some socialism left; jadajada War -> Delusion and Propaganda + expensive, but beautiful and fitting Skyscrapers.  But it was abandoned after Stalin as it simply was not a good mass-solution for the post-WW2 USSR, especially since many of the stalinist  Reconstruction Plans had no intention of being realized, so Kruschev scrapped everything for a return to excessive Constructivism. Why would you put "beautiful" in quotes? Pre WW2 architecture was far superior to Post WW2 architecture. Both for public buildings and housing.  Addressing the changing tastes argument: Neoclassical architecture has lasted far longer, and examples of it will exist long after most concrete and glass structures have been torn down. Neoclassical architecture represents a style of architecture, which reflects the historical and cultural heritage of the places they are built in. This helps to give a sense of character and identity to the places they are built. They improve people's connection with the past of their society and culture. They also make people feel better about living in the city or town that has that style of architecture, because it is aesthetically pleasing. Large concrete and steel architecture does not do this. It deracinates people from the culture and society they live in. It feels stale and bland, and examples of glass and concrete architecture can be found in towns and cities all over the world.  There are many modernist examples of architecture which are private, daring, experimental buildings like those built by Frank Lloyd Wright. They are very admirable examples, but in general modernist architecture fails to create an environment which feels as aesthetically pleasing, as buildings built in older, pre WW1 styles. Making a comparison between 18th Century clothes, and 20th century clothes, is a poor comparison. 18th buildings were designed with a perennial mindset. 18th Century clothes were not.  The modernist trend to remove building ornamentation in the way that it was incorporated in older buildings, is symptomatic of the fact that we as a society in the west, are not as appreciative of aesthetics and beauty in our society. We are far more likely to build a building as cheaply and efficiently as possible. It's a very empty, utilitarian and materialistic mindset that goes into making new buildings, or street lights, or rubbish bins, or street benches. We as a society have lost the spirit and wisdom to build things with aesthetics and beauty in mind. Such is the absolute state of modernity.  Addressing the housing shortages argument: The 19th century saw some of the most rapid population growth in the history of Europe and North America. This did not stop these societies creating buildings with ornamentation, and valueing aesthetics. If think if you lived in a poor, rural, soviet village, in a wooden home with no running water or electricity, you still would have had a strong social connection to your family and community. These blocks may have had greater material benefits for their occupants, but their layout and small size were not conducive to human flourishing and creating social bonds. They were soul crushing monstrosities, built under the materialistic mindset of Marxist-Leninism. It's was a mindset which ignored the non-materialistic needs of human beings.  Addressing the housing becoming a commodity instead of places to live: This is largely true, but almost all the old neoclassical and baroque buildings were build privately. The issue with bland looking condos and apartment buildings that blight cities like Munich, is one of managerialism, and a managerial led society, with a similar materialistic mindset to soviet planners.  Addressing the car-centric urban planning argument: Very true. Post WW2 town and city planning has been an absolute disaster for modern society. There's no doubt about it. This is again, is largely down to the fact our society post WW2 was run by managers, with a managerial political mindset. The way the highway systems of Europe & North America were constructed, is very different to the way the railways were built. I would argue rail infrastructure's cobstruction and intergration into society, was done far more economically efficiently, and less intrusively, than the way car infrastructure was.

Biggest reason for this is mass production. Not even housing crisis is the main problem but just mass over population and the way buildings are made VS back then.  Back then buildings were rarely built in large quantities. A business owner would own his property, wants to develop it, he would get in touch with one of the architects and they would build a new unique building. That's why European cities are filled with unique buildings and even the blandest ones still are unique and decent looking. None of the materials used for the buildings were mass produced either. Resulting in better overal quality.  Taste is not a valid argument here because architecture has never been in the hands of the general public. It has always been an elitist business where all parties both the client and architect are far removed from the average person and what they would want. This persists to this day with large housing developing companies that sprung up. They will just build whatever is the cheapest and quickest. What the general public would want is not taken into consideration and building unique houses where every one has it's own style is not viable in this mass production based housing economy. At least for Germany, there is one more issue. Years of "schwarze Null" (no new public debt policy) were paid for by having too little people working on stuff like public permits. That's why the waiting period to get a construction permit have increased drastically. But, if you request a permit for something you've already build exactly like this before, many steps can be skipped. Thus, as a developer, you need to make a template that lots of people will accept without changes.

The best place to see how the world of achitecture have changed, is Budapest. From the two-tone, simplistic, but practical mass of Budapest D√©li Railway Station, Metro Line 3's old stations, to the yellowish white brick buildings with Antique Greek-inspired decorations, Zsolnay Roof Tiles, to the practical, brown, grey, red, or yellow colored, all-concrete blocks made in the 60s-70s (Khrushchevkas, but with lightyears worth of improvements), to panel and block buildings, the Kofahaj√≥ from 2002, and many other styles, that made an imprint in Budapest. Most of them are two-tone. The modern building style is based around monochrome colors, abundance of glass, 90 degree angles, and neoliberal capitalist building quality. Instead of longevity, money is what was the focus, and brand new apartments are becoming less of a commodity, or home, because their prices have left the wages of the people in the dust. Renting is a hazardous game, because squatting is widespread, or your home's owner can kick you in a moment's notice.

While it is true that many ornamental features of the past were mass produced, there were *way* more small craftspeople per-capita than there are now. In addition to growing populations and car centrism, there are simply less artists/craftspeople with the chops to produce the ornamental features in question. Making these buildings expensive if not impossible to build. Basically, a big first step to get ornamental and pedestrian friendly buildings back is to re-cultivate the small craft and trade scenes.

As someone who‚Äôs renovating a historic villa in Italy, I was shocked at how affordable ornamentation is. Take a window, that classical decorative pediment above it is literally a slab of cement that‚Äôs cast on site in a mould. Wash and repeat per window. This is how ornamentation has been done in Italy since Roman times. I GUARANTEE it costs less than that silly modern cladding that the put all over buildings now.
The tastes of the people haven‚Äôt changed. Surveys have consistently shown that Art Deco, Beaux Arts, and the like are the people‚Äôs favorite kinds of buildings. The people love ornamentation. You know who doesn‚Äôt like ornamentation? Architects. Because at the art schools they go to, they teach them to make blobs, even when it doesn‚Äôt fit with the surroundings. Like the Kunsthaus in historic city of Graz, Austria.  The people hate the international style (the skyscrapers that are just black rectangles, think the UN building or the old Twin Towers). The people hate brutalism. The people hate postmodernism. But design firms don‚Äôt care about what the people want. They absolutely do have money to make pretty buildings. Those blobitechture buildings by celebrity architects cost billions. They could have spent that on an art deco look that costs less.  Also, the Sydney Opera House is expressionist, not modernist. And including the Eiffel Tower and comparing it to a building is disingenuous. It was an accidental landmark. It was intended to be a temporary structure made for the world‚Äôs fair, to be torn down after 20 years. But then people decided it was iconic and petitioned to keep it as a landmark. It was never meant to be a functional building like the Sydney Opera House.  Oh, and Falling Waters was designed terribly. Its cantilevers have been falling apart and it costs millions to maintain it. The cork tiles are causing water damage, since it‚Äôs built over a waterfall. Form absolutely did not follow function here.

When Louis Sullivan said "form follows function" he didn't mean form was less important. He meant that the form should help express what the function of the building is. This became important as cities became more compact and had taller buildings. Sometimes a single building might have three functions and so the form of the building needs to express the function of the different sections. Louis Sullivan created intricate unique ornamentations that gave New York, Boston and Chicago a unique character that didn't simply copy the style of old European buildings but gave the cities a great style that makes them popular to visit today. If you Google Louis Sullivan you'll see close up pictures of ornamentation on sky scrapers that you won't find anywhere else. Form follows function definitely doesn't mean form is less important. And big car centric cities can have beautiful buildings.

Another point is that the *first* generation of car-centric architecture was interesting - it was designed to attract motorists' attention. The earliest "ducks" (buildings shaped like the attraction inside) gave way, with Googie architecture as a transition period, to "decorated sheds" - low-cost buildings with an attention-getting roadside sign, which eventually evolved into ever-fewer national and global brand names instantly recognizable by logo or wordmark.

Another thing I've heard from planners and architects is that the five-over-one is so popular because it's usually one of the easiest things to get approved by sometimes draconian municipal zoning boards. It's blandness is also inoffensiveness, and similar designs being approved by prior councils or other nearby councils makes current councils more willing to approve the project.
I don't know to what extent the information is true, but the change that occurred in Europe and the United States was interpreted as a sign of progress and modernity in other countries.  Several very functional and beautiful buildings in Brazilian cities such as Rio de Janeiro, Porto Alegre, Fortaleza, Manaus were imploded and replaced by others that seemed more like "the face of development, of what is done today in the first world" even when there were no reasons more than real estate speculation or an attempt to conform to "more civilized" standards.  Some architects here also turned down neoclassical or art deco as they considered it "too European, a cultural imposition" on an independent country.

I working in the design and construction industry in Washington DC for 11 years now, i answered this question on Quora too "Is today‚Äôs architecture lacking the importance of expression and materiality?". this video hit on most of the same points i had, but i would like to add that government policy driven via depreciation and variance negotiation is also a major cause. You can get favorable tax credit by building the useIess LEED certified building, depreciation tax benefit, and due to the growing density in the cities, if you tear down the building and rebuild a new one in another 20-40 years, you can negotiate with the city for even MORE tax credit, zoning variance to increase sqft and units, and reset the depreciation with a new building. The government's bad policy and tax incentive is instrumental in causing the "Disposable Building" we see today in urban centers.

Especially the 1920s saw a big interest in those new building styles - Germany's Bauhaus-School (not to be confused with the german hardware store nowadays, they are not connected in any way) created entire village projects with this style. The goal was to provide affordable living spaces for working people (just like after WW2). At the same time, the architects tried to be more than just architects, I can't remember the name right now anymore but there was a very interesting text from that time where they talked about what qualities an architect needs to be a good architect, they have to know about biology, philosophy and many more things to create a living space for a human being where this living being can thrive and live a good life. It's such an important step when it comes to affordable living, the way most of flats are designed this way nowadays have these ideas in them - because if you think back to the 1920s, workers sometimes lived with up to 10 people (parents + children + strangers who rented a bed to sleep there) in a one single room.

Hey, I love your videos and I have some small constructive critisism to give.  I get what you are going for with the framerate on this video. That sort of infographic aesthetic. But you are not doing it correctly. If you look at videos by Vox and all those kinds of channels, they usually have the same slower framerate, wich gives them an interesting look, and they look good because they edit them acording to that framerate. They animate them in a way that the movements have some anticipation (look up "anticipation" on the 12 principles of animation) at the right "rythm" so that it lines up with the frame changes (they probably do this stuff by hand) and for some elements they use motion blur (just the right ammount, you have to use different values for different fps)  You seem to be using transitions and animations that are NOT designed to be used at such low framerates and then just lowering the fps afterwards. This doesn't work.  They also sometimes CHANGE the fps for certain sections or straight up avoid certain movements because those just don't look good. For example, zooming in or panning in full screen (like in the beginning of this video) looks TERRIBLE at low framerates, it looks choppy and disorienting. I personally thought something was wrong with my computer at first.  Getting stuff like this right is complicated and requires a bit of extra work. I'd recommend just sticking to a more traditional framerate to avoid overcomplicating things, that's the simplest solution.  If you did not intend for this video to look like this or it looks like this due to some sort of technical limitation. Please ignore this comment.

The notion that modern architecture is a leftist plot to destroy the human spirit is partly true. leCorbusier designed the Monastery of Sainte-Marie de la Tourette specifically with that aim. He explained it himself- by making the place deliberately oppressive and deeply unpleasant, the monks would have no choice but to focus all their efforts on prayer, and if they didn't, then the very building would drive them mad. Another smaller issue: Historic preservation. Many countries have systems that give special buildings protections, so that the owners aren't allowed to just tear them down. This is usually seen as bad by the owners, since it limits what they can do with the building. Beautiful and special buildings usually get this status after a certain time, while crappy copy-paste concrete boxes usually don't. So why build pretty if it's just a downside?

I agree with some points but disagree on the larger scope. In terms of design there has been a move toward minimalism ( influenced clearly by ikea style mass produced goods, hand made goods are easy to add decoration but for industrial products build by robots adding decoration is difficult). But more importantly i think is actually technology, I work in construction mainly on single family homes where owners could literally do everything they desire but the most sraight forward design is simple and undecorated. Combine that with the need for more thermally efficient housing, think insulation and box style to minimize external surface area and you end up with modern buildings. nice quality decoration is really expensive since it often requires artizans that border on artists. Main thing is cost and taste. Something bland that doesn't offend nobody is a more secure bet than something interesting that might have people dislike it

Good breakdown of the problem. I'd like to add some items:  - Besides material cost is labor cost, both in construction and design. A lot of older construction methods are unionized and therefore more expensive, such as masons. Architects are more efficient than ever thanks to modern software, but all this really means is project timelines have gotten shorter.  - Lot sizes. It is harder to make a building look nice when it takes up an entire city block. Not impossible, but harder. This is also a symptom of neoliberal capitalism and zoning/parking issues.  - Last, my weirdly specific pet peeve: Window muntins are out of style. We need more muntins! They provide nice contrast in density of detail and help make a building facade not feel so sterile.

I make a 3d model of Prague as I love (some) Prague's architecture and I have my own POV . On Charles square, you get a building like beautiful neo-clasicist 4story house on the corner of Zitna nad Charles Square, which needed custom textures, took a month to make and has some 100 000 polygons, and then you have Charles square No 314-317, which are all funcionalist boxey residention buildings with shops in the ground floor, they took two days to make (make a wall with one ledge on the bottom, one at the top, and copy one correctly sized and shaped window on it couple dozen times) and all together, some 120 meters of the street, are some 40 000 polys. Well... these are the reasons why we stopped building pre-modernist tenement houses in the 50ties, but do all these reasons still apply today? 1. Tastes change - yes, but most of us agree these modern buildings are ugly, so.. whose tastes are we following? I haven't seen apt blocks newer than from '70ties that take inspiration from modernism or even brutalism or anything at all. They're in no style, the King is naked. 2. Housing crisis - sure, that's how it started but we can't blame WWII forever. Commie blocks were designed as temporary, built cheap and sold (relatively) cheap, sometimes even given away. Our modern housing crisis is one of getting ourselves locked in a lifetime of debt to acquire apartments below those commie block standards (space per person, space between buildings, distance to important locations or transit). 3. Houses became commodities - but weren't they for most of modern history? Sure, the "pretty old architecture" that survived wasn't build for renting out rooms (mostly). But I think what matters is that we HAD the pretty architecture as well as the cheap ugly buildings that fell apart. I wonder how many beautiful residential buildings of the middle and high class will our generation leave to posteriority? 4. Parking lots - that applies mostly to North America, but I think an argument of 'we'll build it ugly because there's an ugly parking lot across the street" is a ridiculous excuse anyway, once you realise an open parking lot without a building on top is a) even less permanent than modern buildings, and b) a waste of city space.  I guess what I'm trying to say is, explaining how something came to be is not enough (for me at least) to justify why it remained this way for another 50 years. What I take away from this vid is a sad conclusion: developers cut corners because they can, and they can because everybody does it, and housing is a basic necessity so consumers can't refuse to buy. I hope we'll get out of these dark ages of housing architecture someday during my lifetime...

Something unremarked but interesting (and complimentary to the arguments made) here is that there's a basic labour skills and availability factor at play. I'm from the UK and big city CBDs were usually built in the late 1800s, during similar time periods, and have a lot of red brick buildings with tonnes of ornamentation, Georgian terraces etc, but were built at a time where construction was still very labour intensive and where mass Fordist mass production hadn't taken off yet. So one factor in why they're ornamented is that a big population of skilled laborers existed who could just DO ornamentation just as part of their skillset - like how tech companies are trying to get everyone to code so they can suppress coder wages, ornamentation was fairly cheap cause loads of laborers could do it. That's not the case anymore, after a century of automation and mass production. The labour available just doesn't have the skillset.
also i want to ad BAUHAUS Movement influence aka Walter Gropius, Mies van der Rohe, etc . People back then around 1910- 1920 wants something that represent cleanliness. Also internationalism which means no matter where that building stands, it (supposedly) will look ok.

I don't really think these are the *main* reasons, I'm pretty sure it's largely due to the increased cost of construction labour. Back when workers had basically no rights they were so cheap that the extra cost of hiring a big team and having them spend time making a high-quality building in a fancy style was relatively low compared to the material cost. Nowadays, workers' rights mean they have to be paid fairly and things like their safety must be taken into account - this significantly shifts the cost of their labour relative to the cost of the materials (especially considering that modern building materials are dirt cheap compared to what was used historically) and has made people prioritise construction efficiency over quality.

They could build ornate terrace houses like you see in Islington or Belgravia in Grays and Peckham. But the people that can afford to live in those beautiful homes don't live in grays and peckham. The planning team in Grays and Peckham want affordable housing, mostly social housing, that is simple, clean and aesthetically pleasing. The result is a simple box design, normally with a glazed brick, or metal powder coated cladding  of between 4 to 12 stories.   The idea that good/interesting design is shut down by stingy developers who want to save a few buck is bollocks and doesnt stand up to market theory. Spend more on the design, and the property increases in value, the more expensive the property is, the larger the profits for the developer. Why do you think these developments all have indoor swimming pools, private gyms and private landscaped gardens and rooftop members lounges for the residents?   In luxury developments the unambitious box with floor to ceiling windows, brick cladding and grey powder coated steel balconies is chosen because that's the trends of modernist architecture. Thats what people who are looking for a modern apartment in a new development want to see. It makes sense, the box provides lots of internal space, the floor to ceiling windows create lots of internal light and the balconies provide ample exterior space for each resident. I'm sure that somebody mentioned it already, but the ratio of material cost to the cost of labor has shifted massively over the 20th century. Labor is now much more expensive than it used to be and materials were more costly. If you can afford the material, the cost of making it look beautiful disappears in comparison. 18/19th centuries hit the sweet spot for this when materials began to be not prohibitively costly for general use, but labor was still cheap enough so as not to comprise a significant part of the overall costs.

I disagree with almost everything in this video. Also, putting Art Deco and Art Nouveau in with "modernist style buildings" is right, these are both highly decorative styles (it's in the name- Deco=Decorative=ornamentation) they are usually highly respected and loved by lovers of traditional architecture, they were also very rich and diverse in their regional styles and not soulless culture less modernist international styles but were adopted to local culture like baroque and Gothic were in their days. So here's a thought experiment that comes up for me whenever the conversation turns to ornamentation on buildings. There *used* to be manufacturers who would churn out standardized ornamental pieces for buildings just like you described, so presumably if we return to pedestrian-focused, ornamented urban designs, there would be a need for something like that in the future. But what if we wanted ornamentation that *wasn't* neo-classical? Like, sure: the basic elements of ornamental designs -- columns, frames around windows, decorative stuff segmenting the vertical space, elements around the roof, etc. -- would still be there, but what if they weren't based on greco-roman designs?  For example, columns: we still use basically three types of column design -- Doric, Ionian, Corinthian (or some mix of them) -- for our neoclassical columns. What if the tops of columns weren't either a scroll or a leaf shape? What would be a design that reflected more future-looking √¶sthetics? Or also: what sort of decorative elements could you make if you were using CNC and 3D printing to design them, that couldn't be made with molds or by hand? What kind of decorations would be possible?  I'd very much like to see the return of the basic principles of classical architecture, but I'd also like to see a more modern take on the decorative elements that make it so interesting. Something that could retain all the detail and variety and irregularity of classical buildings, without feeling like being overwhelmed by the past.

Our buildings are a product of our values, or more specifically the values of the people paying for them. Today‚Äôs big developer only cares about dollars, so the end product will make that abundantly clear. Back a hundred years ago in the US, when small local developers dominated the market, you had more input of ideas about what is an overall good addition to the neighborhood. This was partly because a wider variety of smaller buildings has a better chance to get things right than one big one all at once, and partly because the modernist concept of questioning everything and favoring new ideas over good ideas had not taken over accounting, construction, or design.    It‚Äôs too bad that the squeezed economic system of extreme manufactured scarcity and misguided zoning regulations means that only the biggest and most cut-throat developers can get anything built.

Ornaments can be massproduced. That's how all the neo-styles during the 19th century came up. You could order stucco pieces from a catalogue and glue them on your fassade. It is entirely normal to reject the dominant style and replace it with something new. People in the 19th century hated Rokoko buildings. There is also a design philosophy now that building for the inhabitants of the building is more important than those viewing the building from the outside. Those neo-classical designs look cool, but the ornamental columns reduce the size of the windows, which is bad for the building's inhabitants, so you get buildings entirely encased in glass. They're also heavy, all that stonework adds weight to the building, which means more support must be added. This can lead to more interior columns which block views, interfere with office layouts, and reduce the amount of useable floor space.

Buildings in ye-olden times were often commissioned by extremely wealthy aristocrats/nobles who wished for a specific design that would reflect their personality, tastes and show off their wealth.  Modern buildings are built by bureaucrats and business people who want to make money and/or fulfil a specific objective.   And as the video says - people wanted something new. YOU like the design of those old buildings (and to be fair, I do as well), because they are becoming rarer and you‚Äôve seen so much of modern architecture, that you desire something new. The people of the early 21st century saw heaps of that old style of architecture and wanted something new - something sleek, something space-agey, something that didn‚Äôt tie itself to the old ways. So they made the new buildings, which looked really new and modern and different for the time.   It‚Äôs not ‚Äúworse‚Äù or ‚Äúbetter‚Äù than the old ways - they changed their style because they got bored of the old style. And now some want to go back, because they‚Äôre tired of the current style. Artists, architects, designers - these groups keep on changing styles ever generation because they get bored and/or they feel they need to make a new style to make a name for themselves.

I'm not sure I buy any of the reasons Adam Something gives for the lack of poetry in our cities. Yes, I call it lack of poetry when  everything built is completely without mystery, completely blank. I grew up in Brooklyn in the mid-twentieth century. I'm talking about working class Brooklyn, poor Brooklyn. And yet I walked the streets entranced. I can remember row after row of brownstones, basically mass produced late nineteenth and early twentieth century housing. Much of it housing for the middle, lower-middle and working classes. It was repetitive but the stone had a warmth about it, the buildings set back from the street with imposing stairways up to the second floor and windows not naked but with the crowns and sills (sorry, I don't know the architectural terms) in other words settings, something understood as NECESSARY from Roman times. The buildings had dignity. Then there were other neighborhoods, truly poor, where the architecture was makeshift and higgledy piggledy, but the very unevenness of it, the little alleys, the trees lifting the slate sidewalks, all of it came together in a kind of unorchestrated beauty. Yes, beauty!  Now. Now the alleged best and brightest think their control, their total control is best. Nothing unplanned. Everything rational. In the meanest sense of that term. I'm almost certain that THAT is the disaster.
