The real reason is just plain economics. Ornamentation is inherently labor intensive, and since labor has gotten more and more expensive (in developed countries that have workplace safety standards), developers and manufacturers have to be really stingy when it comes to labor. This means ornamentation just isn't viable.  The reasons you listed don't really add up. Changing tastes: tastes have changed as long as buildings have existed, but until recently, every style and trend has had some kind of ornamentation. Why should it be different now? Housing shortages: perhaps is a small part of the reason. Still, there have been times and places throughout history that have had housing shortages. What happens is cheap temporary structures are built and over time they get replaced by more permanent structures as the economy grows. Maybe that's part of what we're seeing today, but cheap shoddy housing is hardly anything new. The biggest counterexample is that plenty of expensive, luxury buildings are built all the time, and they're all just as bland and undecorated as the cheap stuff. Housing as Commodity: housing speculation is probably more extreme now than it has been before (in recent memory, arguably ever). Still, money has been around forever, and many people who built buildings all throughout history did so because they thought they could make money off of it. Do you think they weren't trying to do everything to cut costs? Also if it's a shoddy building and it starts falling apart 15 years after it's built, landlords have always been capable of blaming their tenants for any problems! Car centric urban planning: this one might be the explanation that holds the most water. Because of the suburban experiment, housing development does look very different from how it's been since time immemorial. Still bland, plain buildings with nothing to distinguish them from anything else are built all the time in the most walkable, urban areas too!  Sorry for the long comment, tldr: none of the explanations in the video hold together. It's really the result of rising labor costs and how ornamentation is inherently labor intensive.

I'll leave it as an exercise in self-reflection as to why that is the case.

Here in Paris the best buildings are those built 150-200 years ago in the "Haussmanian" style,   built out of thick blocks of lutenian limestone dug up from underneath the city, absolutely beautiful, and nice to live in, also: they haven't moved in literal centuries, whilst concrete buildings from the 70's are already full of cockroaches and falling apart (talking from experience here). Now I live in a building finished in 1894 with walls that are over a foot thick and for some reason those have a lot less humidity and pest problems. Back then people took pride in making things that would last, not in the car they bought with the money they made doing the work. IMO Haussmanian style buildings are perfect for dense cities, they are not too tall and thus don't deprive you of sunlight, and allow for some plants on the balcony at least. It's also worth noting that going much further back, the abandonment of classical and pre-classical architectural specialization played a part in making this inevitable. Housing, and schools, and office buildings, despite serving drastically different functions are designed and have been for a long time with a standardized, rectangular rooms with hallways design philosophy.  You wouldn't see anything like a refrigeration tower (a round room with a big pit in the middle and a roof that converges into a chimney to siphon off as much heat as possible causing the pit to get cold and stay cold) these days. They'll just go for electric refrigeration, ignoring that combining these technologies would make the latter significantly more efficient because it would take less energy to keep the temperature down, at least in hot dry places. The closest you get are theatres and political chambers in terms of designing rooms and buildings around their function, rather than simply incorporating their function into the standardized design hegemony regardless of how inefficient and expensive it is to do so...  I know a lot of humans like consistancy, but it's kinda rediculous to make spaces that are less energy or space efficient simply because they are familiar.    Buildings are tools. Their purpose isn't to be a box, they have different purposes depending on what they are used for. A house/apartment building is built to house residents, to provide them with a permanent shelter (in concept anyways). A school is meant for teaching. A hospital is meant for housing and treating the ill, an office building is meant for facilitating office work, etc. Since these are very different purposes, it makes way more sense to approach them with the question "what is this for?" first rather than second after some standardized conception of building design.  The way it is now is like if every building tool looked like a shovel, but they randomly stuck things onto the head to make useful for a different purpose... A hammer being a shovel with a metal brick on the side, a scrwdriver being a shovel with a tiny screwdriver head on the end, etc. Instead of just giving it a differently shaped head.
To be clear, after 100 years modernism have to go, it has nothing to say anymore. And I'm a modernist (well big part).
Safety regulations as well play an absolutely major role in how buildings end up looking. Part of it is a better understanding of what makes a building safer than another; fire escapes, good ventilation, good heating efficiency... and part is that unfurtunately urban sprawling is forcing us to build on less than optimal places, prone to inundations or -and i'm sorry to say this now- earthquake damage.

Beside survivorship bias, I would also add another reason: materials that do not pass the test of time.  The old buildings that survived until today are made with traditional materials like stone, wood, or bricks. Early modernist architecture (1930s and 40s) as well. And they have a noble patina about them because natural materials age well.  In the latter half of the 20th century the architects started to experiment with new building materials: various plastics, fortified concrete - often raw with no parget or plasterwork, glass, and glass-plastic hybrids. Many of that stuff did not age well, and today it looks dirty and shabby instead of chic. Also, many of these buildings are 30-70 years old now, and in dire need for their first big renovation.

I'm gonna presume you don't like shopping malls either. That's fine. You don't have to. I grew up in a time when they were rapidly approaching ubiquity and now we're in a time where they're rapidly going extinct. But what's interesting - to me, anyway - is that they reflect kind of the same trend you're talking about here.  The first wave of malls around here - from the late 1960s - were a little on the bland side, but the next wave of malls were actually very nice and stylistic. I'm not gonna say they were great, they're just shopping malls after all - but they had interesting architecture, some had art, some even had a large central area for functions. High School band concerts, or the local drama school's not-very-good plays, etc. One about 10 miles south of me had large scale copies of italian statuary all over the place. One near my school had this gorgeious exposed-frame skylight that ran the entire length of the building. Very nice.  Then, around 1990 , they all became more and more utilitarian. The statues disappeared from the one that had them, the one with the cool ceiling was knocked down, the meeting areas were taken out and replaced with skating rinks, basically anything neat and distinctive was removed to make them more bland, and I'm not sure why.
Imagine wasting your time making ugly overdesigned Neoclasical buildings, when you could just make your buildings in the most utilitarian way possible. -This post was made by the Brutalism gang.

Before watching the video:  Saturation and subversion.  Postmodern architecture is basically a subversion of all previous architectural standards, coupled with the occasional tasteful brutalism (if you're lucky). The previous ornamented windowframes are replaced by... nothing. The orange roofing tiles are replaced by grayish black metal. The stone or clay bricks are replaced by empty, smooth, monochrome walls.  The problem is that this subversion has become the standard, so architecture as  a whole became empty.  EDIT:  At 7:30, I strongly disagree. I get your argument that it's supposedly cheaper to make buildings with minimalistic designs, but we live in an era where hard polymer foam shapes can be used to cheaply ornament buildings. If one thing goes against "old school" architecture, it's spacious rooms and high ceilings, but  stylistically there is practically no extra cost to switching the style of a building from postmodern to neoclassicist, for example.
I mostly agree with you, but you did carefully avoid talking about brutalism, which was a case of architects actively promoting building styles that everyone else found and still finds horrendous

2:06 i would if it would be afortable
I think if you look a little deeper, you'd find that in every point in history, most builders built to function over form. The exceptions to these are cases were some sociopath decided that only an ostentatious display of wealth or power will do. Perhaps an autocrat wanted to make some grand landmark to impress the foreign dignitaries, or a wealthy merchant wanted to one up another wealthy merchant. And then the next generation of autocrat or merchant has to one up the last. After hundreds of years, it's not hard to get entire districts or cities of "beautiful buildings". We can deduce this by looking at the building projects that are classified as "grandiose" today, and asking why they are built that way. Why do today's Gulf oil barons build massive skyscrapers? To show off their wealth. Why do autocrats today engage in massive building projects on the public dime? To distract their people with paychecks and other shiny objects to preserve their power and one up the neighbors.   There is a difference between building something to last, and building to show off, though they often coincide because those seeking to show off are looking to leave a legacy. But there is a problem with building things to last, it makes inevitable change all the more difficult. I remember growing up when my dad worked at an industrial facility that will remain nameless. Thanks to numerous counterproductive governmental policies, many of the former functions had moved to other states, and now they were trying to tear down the buildings. But they were having a devil of a time because they were build to withstand a near nuclear blast. There is a balance to be maintained between building a structure that can endure the test of time, and also being able to change with the times, including changing tastes, styles, functions, and so on. And that has far more to do with the bland esthetic of the new "mixed use" developments than the developers being cheap. They know that taste and functions are likely to change far sooner than the actual lifespan of quality materials, so why waste money on quality materials? Besides, an intentional effort to mimic an actual historical style is just going to offend one demographic or another. Babies can't handle spice.   What's going to end up happening is that cities, assuming someone has the audacity to build a new one, are going to be built of generic multistory concrete platforms, like a parking garages, the size of a city block, designed to be continuously redecorated or rearranged. These blocks would be interconnected with pedestrian causeways, with the surface dedicated to motor vehicle traffic, and inhabited by intermodal containers, singularly or in clusters, serving countless functions, from housing, to retail, to office, to workshops, to agriculture, to storage, and so on. The advantage of this is that the business owner, home owners, ect, can own their containers, and just rent the space. And if they want or need to move, they can simply have their containers rolled over to the built in crane, loaded unto a truck or train, and sent off to a new place. Developers, or the municipality itself, are not responsible for building to suit, and are incentivized to continuously build capacity, because there is always a function that it can serve. It is cheaper for all involved, with the builder only responsible for the platform and basic utilities, and the inhabitant can build their holdings incrementally.

I think the term missing here is 'Principle-Agent problem'  Buildings used to be built by/for the people who were going to live in them or use them, or by a mayor/king who wants their territory to be nice.  Now they are designed and built in order to be sold, so the person making the decisions about them never has to live in them or walk past them, so they have no reason to care.  That's a principle-agent problem.

While I agree on most of the points he makes (capitalism, car centrism, tastes, etc.), I think Adam misses the actual reasons why people like (and want to build more) traditional architecture.  The new traditional architecture movement is about more than just building houses like "in the good ol' days."  First of all, the main difference between modernist architecture and traditional architecture is not only the amount of ornamentation it uses, but also that modernism denies the existence of objective beauty. That's where the phrase "form follows function" comes from. If there's no such thing as objective "beauty," why even bother with it at all?  Well, this thinking is obviously flawed because, while it's true that tastes differ between people, most humans still consider similar things to be beautiful. There are studies that show, that people generally prefer "traditional forms" over their modernist counterparts. Traditional architecture tries to use "tried and tested" forms and decorations (e.g. classic column order, the golden ratio, etc.) to appeal to the largest amount of people possible. Modernist architecture can be visually beautiful, but most of the time that's not the main intention of its design (pls. note that "visually beautiful" doesn't include technically interesting stuff like record-breaking heights or gigantic glass panels or objective benefits like large rooms or good insulation).  I found the example with the suit a bit odd. Yes, the suit is a modern garment, but it's still part of classic menswear. I want to dwell a bit deeper into this topic because I think there are a lot of parallels between architecture and fashion. The main difference between classic menswear and modern fashion is not only that its "older", but that garments like the suit stand at the end of the line of a long evolution of European male garments. Yes, a suit is a modern piece of fashion, but details like the lapels or flaps echo the rich history behind its design.  The traditional architecture movement basically tries to do something very similar. It tries to use the wisdom of the past, to build the buildings of the future. It's kind of weird how Adam uses the Eiffel Tower or Rockefeller Center as examples of "modern architecture." Many people consider Art Deco, Art Nouveau, etc. as the last great "hurray" of traditional architecture and art. While the main purpose of the Eifeltower was to be the highest thing possible, it still followed traditional forms and symmetry and even features several classic ornaments on its exterior.  Everyone who knows a little bit about fashion will also know, that classic menswear differs quite substantially from modern fast and ultra fast fashion.¬† Because of their timeless design, a suit or tuxedo can be worn for much longer, so it makes sense to make them out of higher quality materials. This is obviously much better for the environment than buying a new, cheaply manufactured polyster shirt every 6 months.  Modern architecture suffers from exactly the same issues as modern fast fashion does. Most buildings nowadays look modern, sometimes even futuristic, right after their construction, but will look completely out of date after just a few decades. With modern construction methods, it has become quite easy to just bulldoze an existing building and construct a new one after 50 years. Obviously, it's much better for the environment to build and reuse long-lasting, high-quality buildings.  Traditional architecture has proven itself to be quite timeless. Yes, it might not always represent the most avant-garde tastes of the time, but I'm pretty sure there was never a point in history when a majority of people thought "damn that pantheon thing is kinda ugly, let's raise it and build something more beautiful." Furthermore, traditional architecture tends to be better adapted to local climates and is usually made from local materials, which is also more sustainable.  ¬†  Environmental factors, beauty, cultural preservation, and better integration into walkable cities are the main reasons why people want to build more traditional architecture.  ¬†  Oh, and right wingers like new trad because of some dumb conspiracy bullsh*t, but please don't listen to them; they're just a bunch of idiots destroying the reputation of the whole movement.

"Architectural" youtubers seems to be mainly from America and they simply... just... don't understand modernist architecture. And modernism in Europe is entire different story. After the IWW, neo-historical architecture was seen as the architecture of rich capitalists and conservatives defending their interests. Many of the new architects were socialists from the new, young countries. They wanted to build for the poor masses, workers etc., to provide them with the greenery, air and light that the older, 'nice looking' cities were unable to provide. Housing conditions in 19th century cities for most people were ATROCIOUS. And the decorative "nice" architecture looked all the same, from Berlin to Chicago to Buneos Aires do Shanghai (yes, its colonialism). It had to be changed. Compared to Europe, America at this time hardly bothered with social housing, "pretty" decorated art deco skyscrapers were all the rage. Modernism in the form so often criticised on You Tube only became widespread in the USA after the Second World War, when Mies van der Rohe (who, it is worth noting, wanted to make a deal with the Nazis in Germany until the last minute, then emigrated) gave the skyscrapers of rich capitalists a modern, fashionable form. But the socialist aspects of modernism on a larger scale did not take root as it did in Europe. If you want to understand modernist architecture, try to learn about projects like "Red Vienna" or the housing of new countries like Czechoslovakia or Second Poland Republic. It is a story of emancipation and a way out of poverty for many people

I design buildings. It's cost.  However, when international Modernism became in vogue, it was legitimately high fashion, space-age stuff... but it was cheaper than ornamentation. Once that style caught on, there was no excuse to waste a client's money on decorations when all that decoration meant the building would have to shrink by 30%. Also, there is much less pressure now for owners to fit in. Nobody talks, nobody has social pressure to look nice (except for their customers), so when the choice between beauty and budget comes up... budget wins.  It's sad, really.

In Stockholm Sweden there is a wonderful architectural style that I think strikes the perfect balance of cost/time of construction while being very beautiful.  It‚Äôs called ‚ÄòSwedish Grace‚Äô and the buildings are generally 3-8 stories tall, ornate stonework is kept to the first floor, while all the floors from 2 and above are usually a simple plaster/stucco wall, and if there‚Äôs ornamentation on those upper floors it tends to be simple and used sparingly.  This architecture style is so simple and beautiful that Studio Ghibli used it as inspiration for the towns and cities in the movie Kiki‚Äôs Delivery Service.  I think a prefab framework like soviet block housing combined with the extra work of a Swedish Grace facade would be amazing in some sort of Swedish Grace revivalism movement. Another huge reason large stores like Walmart don't customize their stores is for consistency. Having stores be bland and more or less the same is an intentional feel. You know when you're in Walmart and you (more or less) know where to find the things you're looking for regardless of which store you're at. These stores are interchangeable, because it makes it easier for Walmart control variables around product placement, and they're boring so you don't spend time looking at architecture and instead look at products. Walmart isn't supposed to be a place you want to spend time in, it's designed to put as many products between you and the stuff you want, with the goal of getting you to buy as much as possible as efficiently as possible.   Stores that don't do this are usually out-competed by stores that do. Not just because they make more money, but because stores with inconsistent layout's are annoying and confusing to customers. In Canada, The Bay is one of the worst for this. I've probably been to a dozen stores across the country, and the lack of consistency is infuriating. You end up needlessly exploring the store or asking for help because nothing is labeled or grouped consistently. If I need to buy luggage in Walmart (no matter the store), I know it's probably going to be in the back right of the store, across the main aisle from the shoes, and I need to pass through the clothing section to get there.

In the US we also have the problem of cities trying to ram "mixed use" zoning policies down the throats of outdated use-based zoning ordinances instead of replacing those outdated ordinances wholesale like they should. Policymakers also often implement rules to prevent "monotonous" and "featureless" walls on large developments in dense areas, using a basic understanding about facade depth and "visual interest" to try and force developers to make "interesting" looking things.  That's one of the reasons lots of those 5-over-1s have those bonkers facades that seem to change materials and pattern haphazardly and constantly. It's to comply with those "visual interest" requirements.  Form-based coding would likely do a much better, if imperfect, job of resolving a lot of these issues (and allowing for smaller, less corporate-guided, and more unique buildings instead of the massive block-sized megadevelopments that are the mainstay thanks to how expensive navigating all those regulations is (i.e. small landowners can't afford the pricey attorneys needed to decipher the mystical texts)

One more thing... property tax.  When you tax the building based on the value of the building a landlord won't want his place to look TOO nice, because it will raise his property tax and eat up a lot of the profits he could get from charging more in rent.  Charge a fixed price for access to public utilities and the land, and people will be willing to invest more in the building they put on it because it won't raise their taxes.  You subtly shift the incentive structure.  Right now the formula is (Cost of improvement + Increase in Tax - Increase in rent).  By switching to LVT you shift the formula to (Cost of Improvement - Increase in rent) with (Cost of land tax) being a factor that pushes you to maximize rent.  (Of course, maximizing rent doesn't have to mean raising rents.  It can also mean adding a few more floors of apartments, you are still constrained by market demand).
The "far right" argument is that the central government took control of economies from the free markets. This separated the consumer from the producer. Government employees weren't going to live in these places, and therefore did not care how livable they were. So apparently Adam Something is from the "far right."

I spent two years as an architect in a studio that designed shopping malls during the boom years just before the 2008 crisis. Our clients only cared about cost, cost, and speed. They gave almost zero shit about what we do with the facade as long as we keep everything super cheap and extra fast. We were actually free to experiment with the outer looks of the building, but realistically the time constraints and the budget didn't allow for much.
Beauty should not be conflict with functionality either.  The "Gare do Oriente" train station in Lisbon looks great. But you go there, and the cold wind drafts are a pain in the butt.

I was actually ready to be mad at you, but once again you have calmed my anger with truth. Not every building is an expression of art. If this were the case, the buildings of statement would not make a statement. It's important for architects to be inspired and incorporate ornamentation and design from previous civilisations which actually blend in. The human mind requires designs which are a blend to help map their area and create identity. When a space is ready for a new sense of place the urban area can create a new design which can stand out. We'll ignore the awful mess of Dubai... When a place introduces a new building which is designed to stand out it should be done to progress the sense of place. For example, the Guggenheim Musuem. If everywhere had a Guggenheim there would be no point in the Guggenheim. The same can be said with the Eiffel Tower. It is romantic, a grand piece of ornamentation representative of the city. China can copy it, the USA can use it for a decoration, but that does not reduce the impact and beauty of the true structure. Neo-liberalism is one aspect, but the whole of urban design language actually explains the reason for designs being considered boring. Simply, to create a map of the urban area it is easier and more expedient to create simple structures to allow orientation and to enable grand designs to be just that.

One important point I think wasn't touched on here re: neoliberal capitalism is the tragedy of the commons. Having a beautiful atmosphere with good-looking buildings is a surprisingly large improvement to quality of life for people living there. However, the benefits are shared across the population (the people who see the building), while the cost is paid by the people building it. In a perfect world, we could fix this by enforcing that buildings look nice at a municipal level, but in practice that's nightmarishly difficult to enforce and requires a great deal of political will that could usually be better spent elsewhere (especially given the crappy urban fabric a lot of cities have...).

Well there‚Äôs another reason for lack of ornamentation; steel and concrete.   Before we started building on a a mass scale with steel and concrete unless you had a large supply of Iron or oak you couldn‚Äôt build building with larger open spaces.  Since the advent of steel and concrete construction we can have wider rooms, larger windows etc. that tends to change the look of buildings to the point where ornamentation looks ‚Ä¶. Well ‚Ä¶. Two more: Democracy. The old big beautiful buildings were almost exclusively built by the royalty, aristocrats, or the church. Or by wealthy businessmen for themselves (they still build them but they do so far away from cities). 20th century democratized development, opening it up to way less wealthy. And the less wealthy will prefer cheap, industrialised ready-made solutions to hiring their own renowned architect to amaze everybody.  And another thing: taste. What people consider beautiful is usually the thing that's about 30 years older than themselves. My parents hate 70s brutalist architecture, my fellow millenials seem to have a lot of love for it. It's probably because when it's been there for 30 before you, then it was already well lived-in when you seen it the first time. For you it's familiar and welcoming because for you it's the way the world is supposed to be...

Adam sometimes has takes I think are overly negative or miss out on important nuances. This time, I think he's right on the money. From the clear and broad discussion of the causes of the problem, all the way down to acknowledging that ornamentation is nice while also pointing out modernism can be done well.  The only thing I'd add is that a lot of the 5-over-1 style buildings can be seen more as a first wave of new dense housing. We're in the middle of a housing shortage because of decades of restrictive zoning, and these buildings are more like commie blocks than high-quality housing. While they're called "luxury" right now, they will mostly likely eventually downgrade to affordable housing as developers get used to building densely again, and higher wage earners start wanting something even nicer. Right now those higher wage earners are willing to take ANYTHING if it means having access to city amenities.
While I agree on a lot of things presented in the video, I have a few counter-arguments to make:   1. Changing tastes. While the desire to reject old forms and invent new ones explains the sudden shift to modernism after WW1, it's hardly the reason for ornamentation being practically banned for more than 100 years now, especially considering how cherished the old buildings and neighbourhoods are. Consider the evolution of music, fine arts, fashion, film or theatre over the past century: there's been dozens different movements in each art form, each drawing inspiration from all kinds of places. Nobody would call you "tasteless" for composing a new piece of classical music, or one that's inspired by, say, Vivaldi, just because "tastes have changed and we shouldn't look back". And nobody blames Tarantino for making new Western movies, a genre that's arguably been dead for decades.  2. Housing shortages. That's a valid argument in a war-torn post-WW2 Europe with millions of refugees, but it already falls apart in the 1960s and it certainly doesn't work in the 2020s. There's a growing urbanisation trend ever since then and there's still a housing shortage, but it's no different to the extremely rapid urbanisation during the industrial revolution. The housing shortage back then was arguably even more severe than the one today, yet many neighbourhoods from that time period (incl. working class neighbourhoods) are now protected cultural heritage.  3. Neoliberal capitalism. I'm sorry to say this, but this is the only point that doesn't make any sense at all. Well, it might seem to make make sense on the surface, but there's been a demand for cost-effectiveness in new buildings in every single era of human history. This is why they used to repurpose old structures into new buildings instead of tearing them down - architecture is expensive. Always has been. The only difference is that with today's technology, it's usually cheaper to build something from scratch than it is to renovate. That's still no excuse to build ugly, though. There's clearly value in beautiful places, not just artistic, but also an economic one. Late 19th century was arguably more capitalist than today and look at the places they used to build for themselves. Oh and by the way, they absolutely did mass produce ornamented stucco elements, ornate railings etc. and they didn't even need the technology that we have. Think what they could have achieved if they had 3D printers...  4. Car infestation. There's nothing to debunk in this case: there's no incentive to build nice places if everyone's going to just speed past them or use them as parking lot. It's a very good point and an interesting observation. Car-centric urban planning is fundementally broken and it's going to take a lot of work and political will to fix this. Fortunately, there's growing public awareness and discontent which should help move things in a different direction. That will inevitable bring more attention to the overwhelming ugliness of modernist architecture, though.  5. "We actually do build beautiful buildings" - sure there are architectural marvels being built today, but you can't deny the overwhelming public sentiment denouncing modernist architecture and urban planning as a whole. For every well-designed and decent looking modernist building, there's at least 10 bland and ugly cookie-cutter concrete boxes. How did putting ornaments on your houses become a crime against architecture and good taste? A lot of people clearly like that stuff. Again... why don't we build beautiful buildings anymore?   It's not really "Adam Something debunked" since I agree with him on many points he makes, but I don't think it's useful to just throw are arms up and say "that's the way it is, nothing can be done, there are good reasons why we must build ugly".

Survivor bias plays a huge role in this kind of nostalgia. A century ago plenty of builders used cheap materials, shoddy methods and careless design, for the same reasons as all the centuries before and since. Greed, laziness, ignorance, or because that‚Äôs really all they could afford. Only some buildings back then were designed with care had someone who would splurge on the finest materials. As time passed, the cheap buildings fell into disrepair both because they were cheap, and because nobody loved them as much. The cheap ones were the first to fall down or burn down or be targeted for demolition because, again, nobody loved them. The few nice buildings were preserved and renovated and before long became landmarks, if not treasures. The replacements for the cheap ones were mostly cheap and bad too, and had a short life. Not all, a few were special and could last.  Repeat that long enough and it looks like in the old days they only built masterpieces. It‚Äôs an illusion. You can have same proportion of cheap vs quality buildings in every era, yet see few if any crappy buildings from 100 or 200 years ago. It‚Äôs not that they never built crap, but it‚Äôs easy for us to forget they did because we only see recent crap, The antique crap has been replaced with new crap.  Survivor bias.
Actual modernist buildings are more expensive than regular buildings. Neo-classical architecture is old and outdated too. This isn't the Roman Empire.

I think you don't put enough emphasis on how taste is formed by schools of architecture and experts. Research shows a great majority of the population still prefers old styles, so taste didn't change as much as it appears. Read ''Master Thesis AB Topolnicka ¬∑ 2015 ‚Äî Architect's taste: Do professional building designers know better what beautiful means in reference to architecture?'' (free PDF) to get an idea how students develop the 'right' taste. (I recently heard about a student who was promised a higher grade if she would remove a curved angle with a straight angle.) Another point to consider is that rich people like status. In the past they would build expensive buildings because they would leave a legacy. Money was not really a restraint in many cultural environments because social status was very important. Now the architectural elite will shame you if you build with ornament or elegance. Finally it is interesting to see how traditional styles are now left to construction companies who have no ambition to be original or refined. Consumers still demand beautiful buildings but they can't find the architects anymore, so they have no choice but to turn to cheap copies. It is very sad how this combination of social pressure, fear, stupidity and greed determines how are cities are shaped. Oh BOY!  Now this is the arena I left. Let me put on my architect hat back on for a brief moment. Those bigger points you pointed out are great. The one about money is always the biggest one. Everything that doesn‚Äôt give the client money back is axed. You‚Äôre essentially playing with somebody else‚Äôs money. However, let‚Äôs approach it from the people that are required to be on a project after a certain number of square footage. Architects.   The problems I see from a design stand point are that architects are mainly about aesthetics and there‚Äôs usually a clash with the developer since all they care about is money. Architects and architecture schooling needs to do a better job of educating architects on the needs of clients outside of ‚Äúthey want a building.‚Äù This will help drive better solutions not only that but it will make the architect the voice or authority and not be shut down by the developers wanting things to be cheap as all hell.   The other problem is the tools designers use. A lot of the buildings are similar because behind the scenes the 3d programs we use make people lazy and think the computer is going to do everything for them. A lot of these programs do not like anything that isn‚Äôt orthogonal. So anything that‚Äôs not a box is essentially working against the grain. The amount of times I worked as a professional and the reason given for a decision was ‚Äúoh it‚Äôs easier to do it that way in 3d‚Äù is ridiculous! That's because chain & fast food restaurants make more money with real estate than they make pushing burgers and appetizers laden with salt & fat.  When I was young, every fast food place, so-called "family" restaurants and chain bar & grill had it's own silhouette, easily distinguishable from each other from a distance.  But now, the national chains make more money buying land, constructing generic multi-purpose buildings and renting them out to ether their own franchise es or anyone who wants one.  Most new fast-food places around here fold after about a year and the buildings get converted into insurance offices, accounting services or medical weed dispensaries, all of whom are paying rent to the fast food parent companies.
The answer is: Because its easier and cheaper. Here, buildings of middle class families shifted from older wooden windows with wooden frame and iron grills to modern aluminum sliding windows very recently. So I saw the change as it happened. The aluminum windows are not as beautiful and always look the SAME since they aren't meant to be coloured. Another big contributor to the change from wide, ornamental buildings with arches and such, to tall narrow buildings would be internal rebar.  Old buildings needed to be built to avoid collapsing, without internal support. They would use arches and pillars as load bearing pieces. But modern glass towers just have reinforced concrete inside. No architectural marvels needed.  That being said, rebar gives us a better than ever chance to create beautiful, elaborate and tasteful buildings, but it's too expensive, so they wont do it.

Firstly, it IS good that developers are using this newer modular framework that every building seems to have nowadays because lower costs ARE good when the structures are otherwise being built to the same codes and safety standards, and because the new style tends to be MUCH more well insulated and thus have cheaper ongoing costs.  But weirdly, the thing is that this "New Style" is actually extremely flexible in terms of exterior appearance. The facade of this type of building is just a modular set of tiles to partially protect from the weather. It's a wind screen; it's not structural and it's not even waterproof. Appearance is like 2/3 of what those tiles are for, and there are hundreds if not thousands of styles of tiles to choose from. You can get tiles that look like brick, wood, marble, steel, whatever material. You can get tiles that have textures and patterns and all that stuff. The developers COULD be making these buildings look a lot nicer. It might be a little bit more expensive to use a false-brick facade instead of the single color concrete facade, but it's nowhere near the cost of actual brick ornamentation. Plus the tiles can be removed or replaced as needed, providing easier/cheaper repair and making it easy to hide utilities.  I think the additional cost of it looking nicer is maybe part of it, but I think the car infrastructure part is the real issue. Buildings don't need to look good if you never really have to look at the outside from up close. Although, I do think that this style becomes considerably less bland when there are multiple of the same style in proximity and when they use different colors. You can get a more cohesive "Piet Mondrian" sort of look once you have more lines and colors.

The supermarket chain Morrisons in the UK used to build locally inspired stores (and still do to a degree), where they'd match the local architecture. So up in the more rural parts you'd get big sandstone clad stores or old mill and factory inspired ones in post industrial urban areas. They blend in far better than the typical metal box that costs a fortune to heat and cool that most other chains build everywhere.  Also I guess a big part of this along side cost is probably modularity. They can just take the same components and use them everywhere. This is easier to do when the design is rather simple and minimal. Your Walmarts and Tesco's of the world will have a bunch of predefined store modules they can just plonk in practically any space available to them and have it built pretty quickly because it's all basically pre-fab.

My mother was born in 1948 in Frankfurt, Germany. She grew up for about 10 years in a four room apartment, together with her family and three others, so each family (of around 4) occupied a room. They also used curtains to split larger rooms into two to house more people. Despite from the fact that this was very stressfull at times (as families received letters of their deceased fathers, sons etc. due to the war, so there was usually a lot of drama going on which she didn't understand at her age and nobody really explained to her), she also had to walk through the destroyed city and over huge piles of rubble to go to school or go to the public swimming pool. Over the years the situation slowly got better as people were relocated to new houses and the damage in the city was repaired. She still keeps pictures of that time, the damage was really immense.
An issue I have with architecture here in Sweden is that a lot of old architecture was destroyed, which I mean isn't unique, lots of cities in Europe was bombed and destroyed during world war two... Wait a minute, we didn't participate in that one?! What happened? Turns out for some reason it was decided that a lot (and I mean a _lot_) of old buildings were demolished after the war (and primarily in the 70s actually, I think) and were replaced with newer buildings. Now, some of this new stuff is quite charming actually, especially the stuff built relatively soon after the war, it's modernist but not soulless (the term used here is funkis for this type of architecture), but much of the new isn't.  The thing that gets me most though is that it seems so unbelievably unnecessary to destroy old stuff that was already there. There's also something to be said about that you can't build new old stuff (and that goes for culture overall) once something is destroyed you can't get it back, a reconstruction won't be the same. There is an interesting exception to Developers choosing 5 over 1s - look up the Hiranandani Group. They're a real estate developer in Bombay, and I think they absolutely stand out from the rest of Bombay's modern housing. They build mini towns in the city rather than singular blocks, and their buildings are known for their Neoclassical architecture (in Hiranandani Gardens) or Art Deco architecture (in Hiranandani Estate). This often does come at a cost to them, but their beauty stands out so much that interestingly enough many developers stopped building new housing in the common 5 over 1 style in Bombay, and opted for Neoclassical ornamented architecture to compete. It's very interesting, and I'd like to see you do a video about Hiranandani!

9:05 Objection. Actually, modern city planning tells investors to build smaller garages, because the city managers know that a big garage for hundreds of cars = those hundreds of cars will congest the city. Investors want to build garages, as it increases the price of their flats if a garage parking lot is with it. Selling the same flat without any parking space actually generates lower profit.
There is nothing "neoliberal" about the drastic restrictions on building that turn housing in america into a speculative profit making scheme. The neoliberal position is that we should build more housing and let it compete on its merits, which would result in a situation where building better housing is more profitable than just building any housing .

A couple of misconceptions:  1 - The rich always used property to funnel their wealth on. What happens is that the rich found a quicker way to make their property more valuable. Take a look at New York's billionaires row. The developments in that place in the past 10 years are 7 times more valuable than ALL the affordable/middle-class housing that was built during the same period. One crucial detail - why were those beautifull tenements built in the first place? Well, because of "disgusting capitalism". Only rich nobility could afford building tenements back then - factory owners, nobility, bouergeroise, etc. The tenements became a "d*ck measuring" who has the most money to hire the best architects and build the most ornate building. If you'd enter a poor area, the tenements would reflect the status of the inhabitants. In Krakow for example there is a clear distinction between the Old City, where one after another you can see how wealthy its inhabitants were, and the nearby formerly Jewish Kazimierz district, wich before the 2nd WW was basicly a slum. Capitalism was therefore the main factor why these buldings got built in the first place, and now however, as you've said - due to monetary incentives it's the reason why it was abandoned. And socialism won't be any different, because the money problem with building houses won't just go away. A solution to this could only be achieved on a societal level, where people would have to reject modernism to prop up demand for beautifull housing. This is however unlikely to happen. Housing is already as expensive as it is, and making it even more so because of ornaments won't sit positively with the already exhausted buyers/renters.

There definitely is also an element of architects and city planners approaching any form of classically-inspired architecture with zealous hostility. In terms of taste, the federal institute for "Baukultur" (building culture) in Germany does regular studies and surveys. These stably show that around 80% of the general population throughout the entire age range prefer or strongly prefer classical architecture. However, whenever there is a case of people getting to vote on reconstruction projects, city planners and architects try to stop it with all their might.   Just look at examples like Dresden or Frankfurt: in the reconstruction of the Neumarkt area in Dresden there was a design committee set up for all the not-reconstructed buildings. The committee was packed with modernist architects and city planners that used a just about still adhering to a very vague interpretation of their guidelines with unreasonable reinterpretations that went against what the entire project was supposed to be about to force people wanting to build classical architecture on the plots that weren't designated as reconstruction plots to build modernist instead (the most extreme example is the south-eastern plot on Quartier VII/2). Currently a reconstruction and restoration of classical city scape project is being blocked because after years of planning, a modernist in the building conservation ministry decided that the commie blocks that were recently renovated (and now no longer resemble the original state) were in their current state one of the cities most valuable architectural composition and needed to be protected along with the 6-lane street and the blocks' unobstructed view on the historic city centre... Or Frankfurt where the in the reconstruction of a tiny part of the medieval city centre only 15 out of 35 buildings were designated as reconstructions (some as potential reconstructions) and reconstructions were not allowed in the other plots even if people wanted to, those had to be modernist in rough proportions of the old buildings. After the Dom-R√∂mer-Projekt was done and everyone loved it, immediately it was called a once-and-never-again project despite the surrounding area containing completely generic 1950s apartment blocks that have reached the end of their lifespan and either need to be renovated for about as much as tearing them down and building something new would cost, or need to be torn down and replaced by for example more reconstructed buildings (the latter option would result in a lot more apartments becoming available in a city with too few apartments to begin with). But apparently reconstructing 1% of the pre-war half-timbered houses that defined the city's unique look is "completely reconstructing" the city centre and protecting the generic 1950s blocks that there are hundreds of thousands identical examples of and that can be found in the outer reaches of every German city's inner cities, and that were designed as a temporary solution, are more precious than building more and nicer apartments for the people to live in, all inside nicer buildings to look at in a proven to work model for restoring a part of the very heart of a city to the way it looked before being destroyed. Or again in Frankfurt, the "Schauspielhaus" needs to be renovated, under a modernist coat over 3/4 of the original Jugendstil (a sub-category of art nouveau) building is still there and reconstructing it is very popular. There has now been a multi-year campaign trying everything humanly possible to stop this: making the generic 60s glass box set in front of the historic building a protected structure, gaslighting articles in local newspapers every couple of months claiming the original was completely destroyed in the war (there are pictures of it almost undamaged in the 50s) or that there is nothing left of the old building now (you can literally see sections of the old side facades on Google maps 3D view, and the entire original structure encased in a thin layer of glass and steel), stalling implementation of a referendum beyond the point where the city was legally required to respond, not allowing the referendum despite all legal requirements having been met when they stopped stalling (as far as I know legal proceedings against the city re these two points are still ongoing), ad hominem attacks on proponents of the reconstruction etc. I could keep going with projects in Potsdam, Berlin, Hamburg, Bremen etc.  TLDR: Examples of four prominent projects in major German cities were sabotaged or blocked by architects, city planners, and building conservation ministry employees that want to prevent classical architecture being at any cost.

Hey, Adam. I asked ChatGPT the same question, and it mostly agrees with you! It has also a couple of additional points, though: - Environmental and sustainability concerns: Ornate details and decorations require additional materials and resources, which can be detrimental to the environment. In an era of increasing environmental awareness, many architects and builders prioritize sustainability over decorative embellishments. - Changing societal values: Ornate decorations were often used in the past to demonstrate wealth and power, but modern society places a higher value on efficiency and practicality. I spent last year working at a longhouse, an old Croft-Byre that‚Äôs now a tourist attraction. It was built in 1809 and occupied right up to 1968, by the same family. So it‚Äôs actually older than any of the Victorian buildings these ‚Äòtrad-arch‚Äô types are so horny over. It had no running water, no electricity, the toilet was a plank across a nearby steam and the only source of heat and cooking was a central fire hearth that had to be on all year round, the floor was no concreted until the 1930s. Oh and ‚ÄòByre‚Äô is Scots Gaelic meaning ‚Äòcow shed‚Äô, yes it was an animal barn with some living quarters attached at one end. This was the norm throughout all of the country.  Now lest you think I‚Äôm painting a picture of grinding rural poverty, the family that lived in the Croft, were by all accounts very happy, one of the women living there was one of just 34 women in 1911 in the whole country to go to university, and they were local and respected horse breeders. However, when I look at these ‚Äòtrad-arch‚Äô profiles, I don‚Äôt see pictures of my Longhouse, or tenements, or shanty towns or slums of any variety, no it‚Äôs all marble and palaces and frescoes and- this lot are just fantasying about being rich elites, aren‚Äôt they? I personally like those ''ugly boxes'' as much as those idolised old buildings and they are far from lifeless, culture can flourish around them, in Asian cities (which usually have more than one city centre, unlike many western cities) popular nightlife areas are often ones that are around those ugly boxes (for example in Hong Kong or Kyoto), and anyone who is either from those soviet blocks in Eastern Europe, tall project buildings in the US or banlieues in France let's say, or working class neighborhoods in the UK; or at least used to hang around them, would know very well these buildings are far from lifeless (be it for good or bad reasons). Especially if there are places to hang around /pubs, parks, etc./, shops on the first floors and so on (which would not be possible in many US cities due to zoning laws and the way many neighborhoods are built. Also few more notes: 1. some of these old beautiful buildings in British cities are now pretty much shopping/office districts with lots of third and fourth floors (originally made for living) are empty, maybe because there is not enough demand for office space there (covid really helped with that lol), or nobody wants or can afford to live there or these units are held by absentee owners as investment properties. After all stores close around the end of working around, there is literally no one other than workers/shoppers going home, and people just passing through. The street lights are on, and sometimes also lots of decorative lights, facades are amazing, but I cannot picture anything more lifeless when I happen to pass by during these evening/night hours. 2. living in these buildings (old or newly built) is quite impractical, I once lived in one of the tenement houses in Glasgow (not a fancy building, but compared to these ''boxes'', I am sure some of these people criticising today's architecture would like them, even though they were literally boxes made on a massive scale of their era), the walls were too thick, ceilings too high, lots of things were breaking down, it took forever to heat up probably at a very high cost (I did not have to pay for energies fortunately). I now live in a relatively newly-built ''box'' with a short ceiling, better window isolation, and so on, and while my landlord/agent often neglects things, the building is quite livable thanks to it being more functional and recent. If this newly-built block (well, maybe 40 years old) was in the ''old style'', it'd be either in worse condition or more expensive if the landlord was paying more attention to what it needed. Our block has two 19th century fancy-looking buildings next to it (one was some corporation warehouse, one was/is offices), the flats are not much better, but I imagine upkeep of all that fancy stuff that is there (ornaments on the outside), nice carpets, nice railings, whatever, which you would have there if it was newly-built as well. The people I know living there pay much more in rent despite being in the same area, so no thank you, I am not missing fancy ornaments, as they don't improve quality of my life (it is not like I live outside on the street looking at them all day), and I don't miss paying more for heating just to have some nice tall ceiling.

The "commie blocks" in Europe (and not only in the Eastern Bloc) were designed to be liveable cities or suburbs, with shops, postal offices, bank and other service available within walking distance from every apartment. They had relatively large communal green areas as well. With all the modern luxuries of running hot and cold water, electricity and access to a spacious laundry room (often in the cellar, and sometimes in free standing houses) these really helped the house wife in her daily chores. No need to write a note to her husband what to buy on his way home from work (phones? Yes, you had them, but calling him at work? Only if you forgot something important.). Of course, now she could shop during the day.  And for the kids there were playgrounds (as if the trees and the green areas weren't enough).  Cars (in wealthier countries and areas) could be parked nearby,. but they did no intrude on the walking space.   These were far from the soulless dead spaces modern cities present.  Of course, these mass produced houses made in the 1960s and 1970s only have about a fifty year life span, they were not made to last centuries. So now they are all falling apart, maintenance costs skyrocket and the previous landlords do their best to sell them.

Even if the main owner/investor etc, decides to go full ornamentation, increasing the $ per unit area, the "partner" investors might not agree with that: "dude, why "try-hard it" ?" There is a housing crisis, "we" will rent every unit regardless...adding 10% extra cost will wont warrant 10% higher rent, so...there you go. "Oh, articulate every 20 feet this, different color every 40 feet that", all to satisfy the impression some street in Rome or a medieval french village left to that fresh out of school future city official that visited Europe for month or so in his early 20s.  Pre and post industrial revolution planners in Europe "achieved" buildings the size of blocks to look as if they were made the same time or same era by the same hands, now we artificially try to break things up to force the impression that city blocks are a collection of small buildings...5-over-1, but "narrow" is OK i guess.

One thing that should be mentioned is the enormous change in the costs of labor versus materials.   In the past materials used to require enormous amounts of labor to be made, which meant that it was economically viable to have workers sculpt antique architectural features out of plaster, but a building made out of steel beams and glass was unthinkable. Glass was blown and cut by hand, and steel simply wasn't produced in the amounts needed to make beams out of it.   Nowadays we are in the opposite situation, skilled workers have become so expensive relative to materials that everything in construction is engineered to require as little labor as possible to do.   This phenomenon has influenced the entirety of our material culture, and explains changes in other domains too.
No more cheap imigrant labor

Having lived in communist blocks, old buildings and modern buildings - what is apparent in the beautiful old buildings is the extreme amount of labor that went into these houses. Something like that today would be all but impossible to do, building a castle is impossible today. And one of the main reasons is: inequality in society has decreased. Back in the days there was a layer of citizens that were really really rich compared to the people who did the labor. Today, these poor people are rich enough, that - except for a few multi-millionaires who still have amazing buildings built - nobody can pay them enough to regularly make such houses. The rest is simple survivorship bias. The sheds of the poor have disappeared, only the houses of the rich remained. The ancient Egyptians didn't live in pyramids either, but it's all that's left.

Here in the Netherlands at least, aesthetics and a pleasant public space are becoming more of a priority again. As well as climate adaptation, i.e. more trees to prevent heat build up and more permeable surfaces to deal with heavy rain. Car-centricity is also on a steady decline for years now. There was actually a conspiracy though to build city blocks that were purposely stripped of all ornamentation and decoration. A group of very influential architecs set out to create buildings that were 'pure' in spirit and void of all 'distraction' i.e. raw concrete boxes. But this died out in the 80's as these architects who were devout communists were sidelined by greedy developers who brought in architects who would just build for the lowest price. Same level of soul-crushing, but different root cause. And most local governments have since stopped tolerating that anyway and started demanding development that is at least mildly pleasant to be in. Of course a lot of the details that would make it really nice have to be cut out last minute because of budget overruns, but at least the intention was there.
I‚Äôm sure there were lots of ugly building in the past too, they just didn‚Äôt survive until now because surprise surprise, we generally like to get rid of ugly things and keep the nice ones. We still build beautiful buildings. The Market Hall in Rotterdam is stunning. Hundreds of years from now it will be standing, while every Walmart will have been demolished or renovated or repurposed.

Good video, however, you missed a few things.  1. The cost of labour. Ornamenting a building is expensive as it has to be woven into the basic design configuration of the building itself. 500 years ago, public works could be highly ornamental because even skilled workers were barely paid anything. In Canada, the MEDIAN wage for an Ironworker assembling these towers is $41 ph.  2. Also, the height of an ornamented building is largely limited to several stories, as a necessary aspect of the aesthetics of these buildings is their human scale. If you said that the Praha train station had to be 3 stories higher, it would require a complete redesign to incorporate that change in a way that made visual sense. Adding floors to a modern building is just copy/paste. You could take 20 floors off of a modern 60 story condo tower and no one would notice, Or add 20 stories to a 40 story tower. No one cares. 3. Similar to your point about housing as investment is the relation of aesthetics and economics and dominant ideology. To pull a few points from Gramsci and Adorno and wildly simplify them, the dominant ideology is the ideology of the dominant class. In this case, the capitalists. (Lenin had already diagnosed the Soviet system as State Based Capitalism in the early 1920s. This was further exemplified by Deng Xiao Ping in the shift of China to a state regulated Capitalism, basically stating the obvious.) Thus Modernist architecture is valorised not so much as "form follows function" but more that bare "necessity is transformed into a virtue". 4. One end point of point 3 above is in Brutalist architecture, where the drawings look cool on paper and the buildings are ugly, uncomfortable, disuseful, and have more in common with military bunkers than places to live and work. At the same time, Brutalism is relatively cheap - it's just reinforced concrete facades with few windows - windows are long term expensive compared to the upkeep, cleaning, and eventual replacement of windows. Also, to add to your point about housing as investment, Billionaires from China need places to park their money, too, ya know. So a few of them upend their sofas and shake out several tens of millions in loose change each and together they invest in the building of (a) condo tower(s) in Vancouver or Toronto or wherever. Then, they sit on it for a few years, selling only a minority of the condos. This of course, drives the prices up and they rake in millions, while turning the downtowns of those targeted cities into glass and steel dystopias. Vancouver has enacted a few laws to stop this, as has Toronto, but these laws are weak and easily circumvented and have done little to stop or slow the proliferation of these ridiculous towers. Finally , I would caution about this angle of critique. Not to say "Don't Do This" - not at all - it's a necessary critique that is part and parcel of building more sustainable and livable cities. The problem has to do with the political angles surrounding others who have also critiqued modernist architecture. From conservatives like Tom Wolfe (From Bauhaus to Our House) to delusional  right wing nationalists like JH Kunstler. Kunstler I find especially exasperating, because his book The Long Emergency is still relevant and his critique of modern architecture, especially Brutalism, is spot on. He earnestly wants walkable cities, the elimination of car centric suburbs, etc. Unfortunately, over the past 10 years or so, he has increasingly framed his ideas in a weird right wing nationalism that is very disappointing. Anyway, I've rambled enough. Good video. Thanks!

Please stop perpetuating this myth that housing and in general buildings today are built to a lower quality than in the past. In fact the opposite is true. Homes since the 1980s are much more energy efficient, use much less toxic materials and are generally more resistant to decay and breakdown than buildings built in the past. What you are experiencing is survivorship bias.  The older buildings that exist today were the ones on choice land, who were able to build using old growth wood and other higher end materials, and were maintained over time. Homes without this capital were torn down and replaced. All those newer buildings in older cities exist in places where the old building likely was decaying away. Lets not even get started talking about weather resistance, earthquake protections and the such.

When you are designing a high-rise building after you design the first floor and mezzanine, you design the next floor and repeat that until you reach a top. The basic design, list of materials, etc., are pretty much the same on floor 3 as floor 33. Your design costs are kept to a minimum. Finishes are for another architect or interior designer to do. Uniformity is cheaper by economy of scale. While the first designers of box architecture were geniuses in their own way, it doesn't take a genius to design a monolithic high-rise and control costs that way.
The cost of human labor also went up, those orbaments had to dezign, manufacture and construct with ha ds. Its part of the expenses, but worth to mention. That was part of the industrialization of housing, most radical exqmple is premade houses.

The US is facing a housing shortage. Developers expect certain returns and people can only pay so much for housing. The most efficient solution for a housing shortage under capitalism is not dissimilar from the solution under soviet socialism--build cheap, ugly, and cookie cutter. Car dependency is a different matter that is largely incentivized by local governments.  There are of course undesirable outcomes from speculation in real estate, e.g. the recent explosion in hedge fund all-cash purchases we have seen. One of the benefits of making real estate a market should be increased competition leading to depressed home prices, but we unfortunately do not see these benefits (could it be that previous market inefficiencies actually benefited buyers and renters over sellers and landlords?). Population growth, ever increasing expectations for standards of living, demand for larger spaces, toxic materials in older homes, urbanization, loss of manufacturing jobs, etc. all place upward pressure on housing prices, and we simply haven't built enough to keep up. These factors and their change over time in the modern context is poorly examined.  Even under capitalism, housing in this country has not always been so horribly expensive. A better analysis would be to look at that change over time and how it has affected both supply and beauty, rather than looking at similarly ugly buildings from the Soviet Union and declaring them better (even though they would certainly be insufficient today).   The issue of housing shortages and how they relate to beauty is also not thoroughly examined. New York City serves as an excellent example to dive into with its shortages and building sprees that came with each wave of immigration. The brownstone today is considered a beautiful house, but at the time was disliked and considered cheaply built--which is why it is now so ubiquitous. Do they seem so nice today because they have been renovated? Has our notion of cheap changed (it seems far more likely people have higher expectations from the 1840's than lower)? What would it actually cost to "build beautiful" vs a five over one, and what would the quality of the housing look like in each case? In most cases, the brownstone is not even allowed to be built--but the five-over-one often is.  Placing the Empire State building, an Art-Deco skyscraper built in the 30's, in the same class as international style skyscrapers mostly built from the 60's on is also a fundamental misunderstanding and simplification. Skyscrapers are also such an extreme of development that they are mostly irrelevant to the average person's life.  There is a startling lack of nuance and understanding in this video, making it an ultimately more harmful than helpful contribution. You are just a youtuber, though.

I wouldn't underestimate the first point: Fashion  In Germany, before WW1 buildings usually were decorated with stucco. Looks nice nowadays, but back then it was actually quite cheap - as it was mass-produced in factories.  After WW2, stucco was out of fashion. It was even common to remove the stucco (in German: "Entstucken"). Nobody would do this today!  So if we'd have stucco as a new fashion trend and would start to mass-produce it again, I think we could do it. But we don't want to.

We must remember whose tastes have changed.  The broader public still prefers buildings with ornament and good placemaking fundamentals by a huge margin, and there is a mountain of research behind it.  The tastes of the capitalists who finance and manage our built environment have changed away from beauty, not the people who have to live/work inside or walk next to these buildings.  There is also a growing amount of research into why blank walls and poorly-defined spaces create low levels of mental stress in humans, so we should kiiiiiiinda care about good placemaking a lot more than we do, and that will clean up a lot of the modern claptrap we are unfortunately being forced-fed.

The car aspect really resonates with me. I don‚Äôt have a car and live in one of those beautiful city cores. For a long time, whenever I visited friends who live in suburbs, I was dumbfounded at the lack of any kind of beauty. I couldn‚Äôt understand how people could stand living surrounded by so much ugliness, and I felt bad for my friends, not understanding how they could possibly be content. Then I got a ride in someone‚Äôs car and realized what the issue was: I had been biking to all these places. I had been experiencing the audiovisual cacophony of the freeways, the endless cookie-cutter neighborhoods and the artless big box stores at a human scale and pace that they were never supposed to be experienced at. My friends, in their cars, had no idea what a wasteland they lived in, because all they ever saw was a short glimpse in between their cozy homes and the manicured insides of the big box stores.

i also seem to recall that the ornamentation industry was heavily unionised, due to requiring  a lot more specialized labour compared to other construction industries, so i wouldn't be surprised that devolpers basically decided to kill it once they had an excuse (the great depression was the final nail in the coffin iirc)
The inflection point was the MoMA Modern Architecture : international exhibition, 1932. That catalog was widely shared and at the dawn of the Great Depression, business was eager for any excuse to cut costs. The "international style" is defined by its lack of cultural reference (hence "international"). After this exhibition during this economic period, Modernism began to die and Classical was buried. Modern does not equal International, and the public needs to know this. In a desperate gasp for a sense of meaning to architecture, post-modern was born 30 years later, and the masses wept.

Another, smaller factor is also just plain survivorship bias. The past had its own bland, ugly, decrepit buildings just like today. It's just that those were usually first in line to be torn down and replaced, while the well-built, good-looking ones were more likely to be kept around and continually renovated.
